اجازت سے شادی کی اور طلاق دی تو اس کی طلاق واقع ہو گی۔ غلام نے آقا کی اجازت سے شادی کی تو شادی ہو گئی۔ اور چونکہ غلام نے شادی کی تھی اس لئے اب مولی کو طلاق دینے کا اختیار نہیں ہوگا بلکہ غلام ہی کو طلاق دینے کا اختیار ہوگا۔ حاشیہ : (پچھلے صفحہ سے آگے ) طلاق شمار کی جائے گی ؟ فرمایا تو کیا ہوگا؟ (الف) حضور نے فرمایا تین آدمیوں سے قلم اٹھالیا گیا ہے۔ سونے والے سے یہاں تک کہ بیدار ہو جائے۔ اور بچے حضرت عقبہ نے فرمایا وسوسے والے کی طلاق جائز نہیں۔

Commentary
This passage is a composite of several lessons. It should be split into separate lessons for clarity.

Key Terms
الصبي (al-sabi): A male who has not reached puberty.
المجنون (al-majnun): A person who has lost his intellect due to insanity.
النائم (al-na'im): A person who is asleep.
رفع القلم (raf' al-qalam): The pen of recording deeds and legal effects is lifted; meaning no accountability and no legal consequences.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the evidence that the divorce of a sleeping person is invalid?
A) Quran 2:286
B) Hadith 'رفع القلم عن النائم'
C) Consensus of the Sahaba
D) Analogy to the minor

Key Takeaway
Divorce requires consciousness and legal capacity; the minor, insane, and sleeping person cannot divorce.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
6 min
Divorce by a Slave with Master's Permission
Ruling on slave's capacity to divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the ruling on a slave's divorce when he marries with his master's permission, and identify who has the right to divorce.

Core Idea
A slave who marries with his master's permission has the right to divorce, and the master cannot divorce on his behalf.

The capacity of a slave to effect a valid divorce depends on the master's permission at the time of marriage. The Hanafi school rules that if a slave marries with the master's permission, he alone holds the right to divorce; the master loses that authority. This lesson presents this specific ruling with its rationale.

Guided Path
1
State the condition for valid marriage
The slave married with the permission (idhn) of his master.
2
Identify who has the right to divorce
The slave himself can divorce, and the master cannot divorce the slave's wife.
3
Explain the rationale
Because the marriage was contracted by the slave with permission, the rights of talaq belong to the slave as the husband.

Commentary
This passage is a composite of several lessons. It should be split into separate lessons for clarity.

Key Terms
العبد (al-'abd): A male slave.
باذن مولاه (bi-idhni mawlahu): With the permission of his master.
وقع الطلاق (waqa'a al-talaq): The divorce takes effect (is valid).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Who has the right to divorce a slave's wife if the slave married with his master's permission?
A) The master
B) The slave only
C) Both the slave and the master
D) A judge

Key Takeaway
A slave who marries with permission is the sole authority for divorce, not his master.

Talaq — Divorce
beginner
6 min
Conditions for Valid Divorce: Sanity and Adulthood
States the condition for valid divorce: sanity and adulthood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a valid divorce in the Hanafi school and identify which individuals are incapable of issuing a valid divorce.

Core Idea
A valid divorce requires a sane, adult husband; absence of either prevents the divorce from taking effect.

Divorce (talaq) is a serious legal act with strict conditions. The Hanafi matn clarifies that only a husband who is sane and adult can effectively divorce his wife. This lesson examines these essential conditions and the exceptional cases where divorce is automatically invalid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its condition
The text states 'divorce of every husband occurs if he is sane and adult.' Identify the two conditions: sanity (aql) and adulthood (bulugh).
2
List individuals whose divorce is invalid
The text mentions a child (sabi), an insane person (majnun), and a sleeping person (na'im). These lack either sanity or adulthood.
3
Analyze the slave exception
A slave who marries with his master's permission divorces effectively, showing that permission grants full capacity despite lack of freedom.
4
Link to broader fiqh principles
These conditions reflect the requirement of intention and legal responsibility (taklif). Only a mukallaf (sane, adult) can perform legally significant acts like divorce.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقع طلاق كل زوج اذا كان عاقلا بالغا [ ۱۸۹۹ (۱۴) ولا يقع طلاق الصبي والمجنون والنائم [ ۱۹۰۰ (۱) واذا تزوج العبد باذن مولاه وطلق وقع بذلك الطلاق
And the divorce of every husband occurs if he is sane and adult. And the divorce of a child, an insane person, and a sleeping person does not occur. And if a slave marries with his master's permission and divorces, that divorce occurs.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the matn. It states three distinct rulings: (1) Divorce is effective only from a sane, adult husband; (2) Divorce from a child, insane, or sleeping person is not effective; (3) A slave's divorce, if he married with his master's permission, is effective. The key conditions for valid divorce are sanity (aql) and adulthood (bulugh). The absence of these prevents the legal effect of divorce because the individual is not fully responsible (mukallaf). The third ruling clarifies that a slave who marries with his master's permission has the right to divorce; his master cannot divorce his wife.

Key Terms
عاقلاً بالغاً (‘āqilan bālighan): The husband must be sane (possessing intellect) and have reached puberty (adult). These are conditions for the validity of divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under which condition is a husband's divorce valid according to this passage?
A) If he is any Muslim man
B) If he is sane and adult
C) If he is married for more than a year
D) If he pronounces divorce three times

Key Takeaway
A valid divorce requires the husband to be sane and adult; without these conditions, the divorce is legally ineffective.

Talaq — Divorce
beginner
8 min
Divorce of Child, Insane, and Sleeping Person is Invalid
Ruling with proof on invalidity of divorce from those lacking legal capacity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three categories of people whose divorce is invalid and identify the primary hadith evidence (raf‘ al-qalam) along with its legal implication.

Core Idea
Divorce requires legal capacity; without sanity, adulthood, or consciousness, it is void.

This lesson examines the foundational ruling that divorce from a child, an insane person, or a sleeping person is legally ineffective. The ruling is based on the famous hadith 'رفع القلم عن ثلاثة' (the pen is lifted from three). We will explore the textual evidence and understand why these individuals lack the necessary legal capacity for such a serious act.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
Pinpoint the three types of people mentioned in the matn: a child, an insane person, and a sleeping person.
These are the only categories explicitly stated, though other similar groups may be analogized.
2
Locate the primary hadith evidence
Find the hadith cited (رفع القلم) and note its source. The hadith is narrated by Abu Dawud, Bukhari, and others.
The text quotes the hadith: 'رفع القلم عن ثلاثة عن النائم حتى يستيقظ وعن الصبى حتى يحتلم وعن المجنون حتى يعقل'.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how the hadith proves that their statements are not legally effective. The phrase 'رفع القلم' means removal of accountability, so their actions are not legally binding.
This is a direct exemption from liability.
4
Note the additional proofs
Observe the Quranic verse (Surah al-Baqarah 2:286) and companion sayings that reinforce this ruling.
The verse 'ربنا لا تؤاخذنا إن نسينا أو أخطأنا' supports the principle that forgetfulness and error remove culpability.
5
Derive the ruling
Conclude that divorce from these categories is void (batil) and does not take effect at all.
This is the unanimous Hanafi position.

Source Text

نمبر ۳۴۳۲) (۳) بخاری شریف میں قول صحابی ہے۔ وقــال عثمان ليس لمجنون ولا لسكران طلاق . وقال ابن عباس طلاق السكران والمستكره ليس بجائز وقال عقبة بن عامر لا يجوز طلاق الموسوس (ب) ( بخاری شریف ، باب الطلاق فی الاخلاق والکر ص ۷۹۳ نمبر ۵۲۶۹) اس حدیث اور اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ مجنون اور بچے کی طلاق واقع نہیں ہوگی (۴) آیت میں ہے۔ ربنا لا تؤاخذنا ان نسينا او اخطانا ( آیت ۲۸۶ سورة البقرة ۲) اس آیت میں ہے اعتبار نہیں ہوگا۔ اور یہی حال سونے والے کا ہے۔ اثر میں ہے عن ابن عباس قال لا يجوز طلاق الصبي (مصنف ابن ابی هیت ۳۲ ما قالوا فی الصحی ج رابع میں ۷۶، نمبر ۱۷۹۲۹) ۱۹۰۰ (۱۶) اگر غلام نے مولی کی اجازت سے شادی کی اور طلاق دی تو اس کی طلاق واقع ہو گی۔ غلام نے آقا کی اجازت سے شادی کی تو شادی ہو گئی۔ اور چونکہ غلام نے شادی کی تھی اس لئے اب مولی کو طلاق دینے کا اختیار نہیں ہوگا بلکہ غلام ہی کو طلاق دینے کا اختیار ہوگا۔
And the divorce of a child, an insane person, and a sleeping person does not occur. And if a slave marries with his master's permission and divorces, that divorce occurs. Sahih Muslim no. 5252... From this hadith it is known that divorce given during menstruation is counted. And if he does not take her back, she will become bain after completing iddah. [1898] (14) The divorce of every husband who is sane and adult occurs. Without sanity and adulthood, contracts and dissolutions do not occur, and the Shariah does not consider them. The hadith is in the next number. [1899] (15) Therefore, the divorce of a child, insane, and sleeping person does not occur. (1) Children and insane persons do not have intellect, so their divorce does not occur. (2) In the hadith: Lifting of the pen from three: from the sleeping until he wakes, from the child until he reaches puberty, from the insane until he regains sanity. (Abu Dawud no. 4403, Bukhari no. 5269, Nasa'i no. 3432) (3) In Bukhari is the saying of a companion: Uthman said: No divorce for an insane or intoxicated person. Ibn Abbas said: The divorce of an intoxicated or coerced person is not valid. Uqbah ibn Amir said: The divorce of a man with waswas is not valid. (Bukhari no. 5269) From this hadith and athar it is known that the divorce of an insane and child does not occur. (4) The verse: 'Our Lord, do not take us to account if we forget or err.' (Surah al-Baqarah 2:286) From this it is known that actions done from forgetfulness or error are not considered. And the actions of a child and insane are out of forgetfulness, so their divorce is not considered. And the same applies to the sleeping person. In the athar from Ibn Abbas: The divorce of a child is not valid. (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba no. 17929) 1900 (16) If a slave married with his master's permission and divorces, his divorce occurs. Since the slave married with the master's permission, the marriage occurred. And because the slave performed the marriage, the master does not have the right to divorce; only the slave has that right.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof, explaining why divorce from a child, insane, or sleeping person is invalid. The reasoning is that these individuals lack legal capacity (ahliyyah) due to lack of intellect or consciousness. The hadith of 'رفع القلم' (lifting of the pen) is cited, which states that accountability is lifted from three: the sleeping until awake, the child until puberty, and the insane until sanity. This establishes that their actions are not legally effective. Additionally, the verse from Surah al-Baqarah (2:286) is used to show that forgetfulness and error remove culpability, and since children and insane act without full awareness, their divorce is not valid. The commentary also includes athar (sayings of companions) supporting this. The passage then transitions to the ruling about the slave's divorce, but that will be treated as a separate lesson.

Key Terms
رفع القلم (raf‘ al-qalam): Lifting of the pen means removal of legal accountability and responsibility for actions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

Talaq — Divorce

Divorce by a Slave Married with Master's Permission is Valid
Ruling statement on slave's divorce validity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that a slave who marries with his master's permission has the authority to issue a valid divorce.

Core Idea
A slave's divorce is effective when he married with his master's consent.

This short lesson clarifies a specific scenario: when a slave marries with his master's permission, his marriage is valid and he retains the right to divorce. The master cannot divorce on his behalf. This ruling is based on the general principle that a person who enters a contract with full capacity (even if a slave) has the corresponding power of dissolution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
A slave marries a woman with explicit permission from his master.
Permission is key; without it, the marriage may be invalid.
2
Note the ruling
The slave's divorce is valid and takes effect.
It is sahih (valid) and not batil.
3
Understand the reason
Since the slave had the authority to contract the marriage (as an agent of the master for that purpose), he also has the authority to dissolve it.
The master delegated the right to marry, which includes the right to divorce.
4
Contrast with master's inability
The master cannot divorce the slave's wife because the marriage was contracted by the slave himself.
The master is not a party to the marriage.

Talaq — Divorce
beginner
6 min
Slave's Divorce with Master's Permission
Ruling with proof: slave's divorce right

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on a slave's divorce when he married with his master's permission and identify the proof from hadith and athar.

Core Idea
The right to divorce belongs to the one who performed the marriage, not the one who gave permission.

Marriage and divorce rights can be affected by the status of the parties. When a slave marries with his master's permission, who holds the right to divorce? This lesson examines the clear ruling from the Prophet's hadith and the practice of Ibn Umar, affirming that the slave, not the master, controls the divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the ruling
Recognize the three parties: master, slave, and slave's wife. The ruling states that the slave's divorce is valid (sahih) and the master's divorce is void (batil).
The condition is that the slave married with his master's permission.
2
Analyse the first proof (hadith)
Read the hadith from Ibn Abbas: the Prophet said 'inna aṭ-ṭalāqa liman akhadha bis-sāq' (divorce belongs to the one who took the calf). Understand that 'akhadha bis-sāq' idiomatically means the one who performed the marriage contract.
The hadith is reported in Ibn Majah and Dar Qutni.
3
Analyse the second proof (athar)
Consider the athar of Ibn Umar: 'Whoever permits his slave to marry, the divorce is in the hand of the slave, not anyone else.' This reinforces the same principle.
The athar is from Sunan al-Bayhaqi.
4
Note the condition and its implication
The ruling applies only when the slave marries with permission. If the slave marries without permission, different rules may apply. The permission does not transfer the right to divorce to the master.
The master's permission enables the marriage but does not give him authority over divorce.
5
Summarise the thamarah (practical consequence)
In practice, the slave exercises his own right to divorce. The master cannot intervene even though he permitted the marriage.
This protects the slave's marital autonomy within the bounds of his status.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واذا تزوج العبد باذن مولاه وطلق وقع بذلك الطلاق ... غلام نے آقا کی اجازت سے شادی کی تو شادی ہو گئی۔ اور چونکہ غلام نے شادی کی تھی اس لئے اب مولی کو طلاق دینے کا اختیار نہیں ہوگا بلکہ غلام ہی کو طلاق دینے کا اختیار ہوگا۔ حدیث میں اس کی تفصیل ہے۔ عن ابن عباس قال اتى النبي الله رجل فقال يا رسول الله ! ان سیدی زوجنی امته وهو يريد ان يفرق بيني وبينها ، قال فصعد رسول الله المنبر فقال يا ايها الناس ما بال احدكم يزوج عبده امته ثم يريد ان يفرق بينهما ؟ انما الطلاق لمن اخذ بالساق (الف) (ابن ماجہ شریف، باب طلاق العبد ص ۲۹۹ نمبر ۲۰۸۱ / دار قطنی ، کتاب الطلاق ج رابع ص ۲۴ نمبر ۳۹۴۶) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ جس نے شادی کی اسی کو طلاق دینے کا حق ہے (۲) اثر میں ہے۔ ان ابن عمر كان يقول من اذن لعبده ان ينكح فالطلاق بيد العبد، ليس بيد غيره من طلاقه شيء (ب) (سن للبیہقی ، باب طلاق العبد بغیر اذن سیدہ ج سابع ،ص۵۹۰، نمبر ۱۵۱۱۴) اس اثر سے بھی معلوم ہوا کہ طلاق کا اختیار غلام کو ہے مولی کو نہیں۔ (۱۹۰۱) (۱۷) اور نہیں واقع ہوگی آقا کی طلاق غلام کی بیوی پر ۔ آقا کی اجازت سے غلام کی شادی تو ہوئی لیکن اب طلاق دینے کا مجاز آقا نہیں رہا غلام خود ہو گا۔
And if a slave marries with his master's permission and divorces, that divorce occurs. ... The slave married with his master's permission, so the marriage occurred. And because the slave performed the marriage, the master does not have the right to divorce; only the slave has that right. The hadith explains this: Ibn Abbas narrated that a man came to the Prophet and said, 'O Messenger of Allah, my master married me to his slave girl and now wants to separate us.' The Prophet ascended the pulpit and said, 'O people, what is the matter with you that you marry your male slave to your female slave and then want to separate them? Divorce belongs to the one who takes the calf (i.e., who performed the marriage).' (Ibn Majah no. 2081, Dar Qutni no. 3946) From this hadith it is known that the right to divorce belongs to the one who married. (2) In the athar: Ibn Umar used to say: Whoever permits his slave to marry, the divorce is in the hand of the slave, not in anyone else's hand. (Sunan al-Bayhaqi no. 15114) From this athar it is also known that the authority of divorce belongs to the slave, not the master. (1901) (17) And the master's divorce does not occur on his slave's wife. The slave's marriage was contracted with the master's permission, but now the master is not authorized to divorce; the slave himself will do so.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof, explaining the case of a slave who marries with his master's permission. Two proofs are cited: First, a hadith where the Prophet (ﷺ) states 'الطلاق لمن اخذ بالساق' (divorce belongs to the one who took the calf) – a metaphor for the one who performed the marriage. Second, the practice of Ibn Umar (ra) who affirmed that the slave holds the right to divorce. Thus, the master cannot divorce the slave's wife, even though he permitted the marriage. The ruling is explicit: the slave's divorce is effective, and the master's divorce is not.

Key Terms
أخذ بالساق (akhadha bis-sāq): Literally 'took the calf'; idiom meaning the one who consummated the marriage or performed the contract.

Key Takeaway
The right to divorce belongs solely to the one who performed the marriage contract; the master's permission does not transfer that right.

The Invalidity of a Master's Divorce and Explicit Divorce Defined
Ruling statement: invalidity of master's divorce and definition of sarih divorce.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that a master's divorce on his slave's wife is invalid and identify the explicit words (sarih) of divorce recognized by the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
A master has no authority over his slave's wife; only the husband can initiate divorce.

This passage establishes a crucial limitation: a master cannot divorce his slave's wife. It then categorizes divorce into two types: explicit (sarih) and implicit (kinayah). Understanding this classification is foundational for correctly applying divorce rulings, as explicit words require no intention and effect an immediate revocable divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main ruling
Read the opening phrase: 'ولا يقع طلاق مولاه على امرأته' (the divorce of his master does not occur on his wife). Recognize that this is a clear ruling of invalidity.
The master has no marital authority over the slave's wife.
2
Classify the types of divorce
Notice the phrase 'والطلاق على ضربين صريح و كناية' (divorce is of two types: explicit and implicit). Memorize this bipartite division.
This classification is key to knowing when intention matters.
3
Define explicit (sarih) divorce
Focus on 'فالصريح قوله انت طالق ومطلقة وطلقتك' (explicit is his saying: 'you are divorced', 'divorced', 'I have divorced you'). These words are clear and unambiguous.
Hanafis consider these words sarih; other schools may vary slightly.
4
Understand the legal effect
The text concludes 'فهذا يقع به الطلاق' (so by this divorce occurs). Explicit words effect a revocable divorce (talāq raj‘ī) without need for intention.
Contrast with kinayah, which requires intention.
5
Apply to a scenario
Imagine a master says 'ant tāliq' to his slave's wife. According to this passage, no divorce occurs. If the slave himself says 'ant tāliq', divorce occurs immediately.
The identity of the speaker is decisive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يقع طلاق مولاه على امرأته [۱۹۰۲ (۱۸) والطلاق على ضربين صريح و كناية فالصريح قوله انت طالق ومطلقة وطلقتك فهذا يقع به الطلاق
And the divorce of his master does not occur on his wife. And divorce is of two types: explicit and implicit. So explicit is his saying: 'You are divorced', 'divorced', and 'I have divorced you', so by this divorce occurs.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the matn. It first confirms that the master's divorce does not affect the slave's wife, as established earlier. Then it introduces the classification of divorce: sarih (explicit) and kinayah (implicit). Explicit divorce uses clear words like 'ant tāliq' (you are divorced), 'mutallaqa' (divorced), or 'tallaqtuki' (I have divorced you). These words result in a revocable divorce (talāq raj‘ī) without need for intention.

Key Terms
صريح (ṣarīḥ): Explicit words that clearly indicate divorce without needing intention, such as 'tāliq' or 'tallatuka'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Two Types of Divorce: Explicit and Implicit
Introduces the classification of divorce into sarih and kinayah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between sarih (explicit) and kinayah (implicit) divorce words, and state the Hanafi ruling that explicit words effect divorce immediately without need for intention.

Core Idea
Explicit divorce words cause divorce instantly; implicit words require intention or context.

In Hanafi law, the words used for divorce fall into two categories: clear (sarih) and ambiguous (kinayah). The legal effect depends entirely on which category the word belongs to. This lesson explains the first category and its immediate ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
The passage states that divorce is of two types: sarih (explicit) and kinayah (implicit). Recognize that this is a classification based on the clarity of the words used.
2
List the explicit words
The passage gives three explicit words: 'ant tāliq' (you are divorced), 'mutallaqa' (divorced), and 'tallaqtuki' (I have divorced you). Memorize these as the standard sarih expressions.
Hanafi fiqh limits sarih words to these and their derivatives.
3
Understand the ruling for sarih
The passage says 'so by this divorce occurs'. This means when a husband uses any of these explicit words, a revocable divorce (talāq raj‘ī) takes effect immediately, regardless of his intention.
Intention is not required because the words are unambiguous.
4
Contrast with kinayah
Although not detailed here, kinayah words (e.g., 'go to your family') do not cause divorce unless accompanied by intention or contextual evidence. This lesson sets the stage for that distinction.
Kinayah will be covered in a subsequent lesson.
5
Apply the ruling
Given a scenario where a husband says one of the sarih phrases, you should be able to state that a valid divorce has occurred. If he uses a non-sarih phrase, you cannot assume divorce without further analysis.
Practical application: always check whether the word is from the sarih list.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والطلاق على ضربين صريح و كناية فالصريح قوله انت طالق ومطلقة وطلقتك فهذا يقع به الطلاق
And divorce is of two types: explicit and implicit. So explicit is his saying: 'You are divorced', 'divorced', and 'I have divorced you', so by this divorce occurs.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling. It defines the two types of divorce: sarih (explicit) and kinayah (implicit). Explicit divorce uses words that have no meaning other than divorce, such as 'ant tāliq' (you are divorced), 'mutallaqa' (divorced), or 'tallaqtuki' (I have divorced you). When a husband uses these words, divorce takes effect immediately without need for intention. Kinayah, which will be discussed later, uses words that could imply divorce but require intention or context.

Key Terms
صريح (ṣarīḥ): Explicit divorce words that have no meaning other than divorce, e.g., 'ant tāliq'.
كناية (kināyah): Implicit words that need intention or context to effect divorce, e.g., 'go to your family'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) 'Go to your mother's house'
C) 'I don't want you'
D) 'You are free'

Key Takeaway
Sarih (explicit) divorce words effect divorce immediately without need for intention; all other words are kinayah and require additional proof.

Master's Divorce Does Not Affect Slave's Wife
Ruling that a master's divorce does not affect his slave's wife, with proofs from hadith and athar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a master's divorce does not affect his slave's wife and explain the proof from hadith and athar.

Core Idea
Divorce belongs only to the husband—the master has no authority over a slave's marriage.

A common assumption is that the master, having arranged the marriage of his slave, retains the right to dissolve it. However, the Hanafi school, based on clear prophetic narration and companion practice, holds that only the slave husband can divorce. This lesson explores the proofs and implications of this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act in question is the master pronouncing divorce on his slave's wife. The ruling is that such a divorce does not fall (la yaqa).
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites a hadith from Ibn Abbas and an athar from Ibn Umar. Identify the primary proof.
The hadith is from Ibn Majah and Dar Qutni.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The operative phrase is 'الطلاق لمن أخذ بالساق' — divorce belongs to the one who holds the leg (the husband).
This shows the right of divorce is tied to the husband, not the master.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
No significant intra-Hanafi or inter-madhhab dispute is recorded. The ruling is agreed upon.
The cross_madhhab_status is muttafaq alayh.
5
Apply the ruling
Thus, if a master attempts to divorce his slave's wife, it has no effect. Only the slave husband can divorce.
This is the mufta bihi position.

Divorce Types: Sarih and Kinayah
Definition and examples of explicit and implicit divorce terms, with Qur'anic and hadith evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish sarih from kinayah divorce terms and state the Hanafi ruling on each.

Core Idea
Sarih divorce terms take effect automatically; kinayah terms require intention to count.

Divorce in Islam can be pronounced using explicit or implicit words. The Hanafi school classifies terms into sarih (explicit) and kinayah (implicit). This lesson explains the definitions, examples, and the evidence from Quran and Sunnah that support the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Recognize that divorce terms are divided into sarih (explicit) and kinayah (implicit) according to Hanafi scholars.
The source states: 'طلاق دو قسموں پر ہے صریح اور کنایہ'.
2
Understand sarih terms
Sarih terms clearly indicate divorce without need for intention. Examples: 'You are divorced' ('تتجے طلاق ہے'), 'I have divorced you' ('میں نے تمہیں طلاق دی').
The text says 'ان سے طلاق کا صاف پتہ چلتا ہے' (they clearly indicate divorce).
3
Understand kinayah terms
Kinayah terms have multiple meanings and require intention or contextual indication to effect divorce. Example: 'Go to your family'.
The source explains that if the husband intends divorce or the context demands it, divorce occurs.
4
Examine the Quranic evidence
The Quranic verse (2:228) uses the word 'الطلاق' which is an explicit term, supporting the classification of sarih.
Verse: 'الطلاق مرتان فإمساك بمعروف أو تسريح بإحسان'.
5
Analyze the hadith of Ibn Umar
The hadith shows that a sarih divorce uttered during menstruation counts as one revocable divorce, confirming automatic effect.
Ibn Umar divorced his wife while she was menstruating; the Prophet told him to take her back, and it counted as one.

Commentary
This passage defines the two types of divorce: sarih (explicit) and kinayah (implicit). Explicit terms clearly indicate divorce, so intention is not needed; implicit terms have multiple meanings and require intention or contextual indication. The text provides the Qur'anic verse (2:228) as evidence that words like 'talaq' are used explicitly. It also cites the hadith of Ibn Umar to show that explicit divorce results in one revocable divorce, even if pronounced during menstruation.

Key Terms
صريح (sarih): Explicit divorce term that clearly indicates divorce without needing intention.
كنایہ (kinayah): Implicit term that may indicate divorce only with intention or contextual clues.

Sarih Divorce: No Intention Required, Only One Falls
Ruling that explicit divorce counts as one revocable even if intending more, and does not require intention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on sarih divorce and apply it to scenarios where intention differs from utterance.

Core Idea
With explicit divorce words, the count is one regardless of intention, and intention is not required at all.

The Hanafi school distinguishes sharply between sarih (explicit) and kinayah (allusive) divorce terms. For sarih terms like 'I divorce you', the mere utterance effects one revocable divorce, no matter what the husband intended. This lesson unpacks the ruling and its proof from the athar of the Salaf, showing that intention is irrelevant in clear matters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? Using explicit divorce words (sarih). What is the hukm? One revocable divorce occurs, and no intention is needed.
Ruling applies only to sarih, not kinayah.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites two athar: from Sha'bi and Hasan al-Basri. Read them in Arabic and translation.
These are Qawl Sahabi, zanni thubut but qati dalala for the Hanafi school.
3
Interpret the wajh al-dalalah
Sha'bi's statement: 'Intention is in what is hidden; as for what is apparent, there is no intention in it.' This distinguishes between sarih (apparent) and kinayah (hidden). Hasan's statement directly rules that saying one divorce while intending three results in only one.
The explicit words are 'apparent', so intention is irrelevant.
4
Contrast with kinayah
For kinayah words, intention is required to determine if divorce occurred and how many. This highlights the importance of classifying divorce terms.
Kinayah: e.g. 'You are on your own' – needs niyyah.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
In the Shafi'i school, some hold that if one intends three with sarih words, three divorces occur because the lafz can carry multiple counts if intended. The Hanafi reliance on the athar rejects this.
Thamarah: practical difference in the number of divorces.

[1903] (19) And only one divorce falls, even if he intended more than that. With these words only one divorce occurs, even if he intended more. Intention is considered in kinayah words, not in sarih words. Athar from Sha'bi: intention is in what is hidden; as for what is apparent, there is no intention in it. Athar from Hasan: a man who divorces his wife once intending three, he said it is one. 1904 (20) These words do not need intention. In sarih words, there is no need for intention to effect divorce; rather, upon uttering them, divorce occurs without intention.

Commentary
This passage contains two rulings: (1903) that using explicit divorce terms results in only one revocable divorce, regardless of how many the husband intended; and (1904) that explicit terms do not require the husband to have the intention of divorce—the words themselves effect it. The proofs are athars from Sha'bi and Hasan al-Basri. Sha'bi states that intention is relevant only for ambiguous matters, not clear ones. Hasan states that if a man says 'you are divorced' once but intends three, only one divorce occurs. The Urdu commentary reinforces that for explicit terms, intention is irrelevant.

Key Terms
نية (niyyah): Intention that is required for kinayah divorce but not for sarih.

Ruling on Phrases Like 'Anti al-Talaq'
Ruling on specific hybrid explicit/implicit phrases requiring intention for number.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for the phrases 'anti al-talaq', 'anti taliq al-talaq', and 'anti taliq taliq', and apply the principle that these phrases require intention for the number of divorces.

Core Idea
'Anti al-talaq' is explicit but ambiguous in number; intention determines whether one or three divorces occur.

The Hanafi school classifies talaq phrases as either sarih (explicit) or kinayah (implicit). However, certain hybrid phrases like 'anti al-talaq' (you are divorce itself) sit in between. This lesson examines the ruling for such phrases, which require intention for the number of divorces—unlike the plain 'anti taliq' which is fully explicit.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase
Recognize that 'anti al-talaq', 'anti taliq al-talaq', and 'anti taliq taliq' are being treated as a distinct category from plain 'anti taliq'.
These phrases are considered sarih in essence but ambiguous in number.
2
Determine the default ruling
When no intention regarding number is present, one revocable divorce (talāq rajʿī) is effective.
This is the default regardless of the wording.
3
Apply the intention rule
If the husband intends two divorces, only one occurs; if he intends three, three occur.
Intention for two is ineffective; the choices are one or three.
4
Compare with 'anti taliq'
Understand why plain 'anti taliq' does not require intention for number (it always yields one revocable divorce unless explicit three is stated).
The difference stems from the grammatical structure of the phrase.
5
Note the practical implication
The number of divorces is either one or three; never two. This protects against unintended triple divorce.
The husband must be careful with intention.

Divorce occurs with the form of the active participle, e.g., 'anti taliq', and since the word is explicit, one revocable divorce occurs. But ...

Commentary
This passage provides a nuanced ruling for phrases like 'anti al-talaq' (you are divorce itself) and 'anti taliq al-talaq' (you are a divorcée of divorce). These are not plain 'anti taliq'. The ruling distinguishes based on intention: if no intention, one revocable divorce; if intention of two, still only one (because explicit words can only give one?); if intention of three, then three divorces occur. This shows that certain phrases combine characteristics of sarih and kinayah. The Urdu commentary attempts to explain, referencing earlier hadith, but the key is that these specific phrases are treated differently from the standard 'anti taliq'.

Key Terms
انت الطلاق (anti al-talaq): A phrase where the wife is called 'divorce itself' – considered explicit but with ambiguity about number.

Explicit Divorce: Intention Does Not Increase Count
Ruling on explicit divorce with proof from Companion sayings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on explicit divorce and apply the principle that intention does not affect the number of divorces in sarih talaq.

Core Idea
Explicit divorce words (sarih) produce only one revocable divorce, regardless of the husband's intention for more.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on explicit divorce (sarih talaq). When a husband uses clear, unambiguous divorce words, his hidden intention about the number does not change the outcome. Only one revocable divorce occurs, even if he intended three. The ruling is supported by statements from early authorities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is pronouncing explicit divorce words (sarih). The ruling is that only one revocable divorce occurs, regardless of intended number.
Sarih words have no alternative meaning.
2
Understand the role of intention
In sarih, intention does not affect the count; the words alone determine the divorce. Intention is only relevant in kinayah (ambiguous) words.
This is the key distinction.
3
Examine the dalil
The text cites two athar: al-Sha'bi states intention is only for hidden matters; al-Hasan ruled that intending three still yields one divorce.
Both athar are from Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah.
4
Derive the principle
Intention is not considered in explicit divorce because the words are clear and independent of hidden intent.
5
Note the practical consequence
A husband cannot turn a single revocable divorce into three irrevocable ones by merely intending.
This prevents manipulation.

And it does not occur except one, even if he intends more than that. And it will not occur except one, even if he intended more than that. From these words, only one divorce occurs, even if he intended more. Consideration of intention is in kinayah words; in explicit words, intention is not considered. (2) There is an athar: from al-Sha'bi, he said: 'Intention is in what is hidden; as for what is apparent, there is no intention in it.' (b) (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah, what they said about a man divorcing his wife once intending three, volume 4, p. 115, no. 18361) From this athar it is understood that in explicit words, intention is not considered. (3) From al-Hasan regarding a man who divorced his wife one divorce intending three: he said, 'It is one.' (c) (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah, what they said about a man divorcing his wife once intending three, volume 4, p. 115, no. 18362) From this athar it is understood that in explicit words, even if he intends three, three do not occur; only one occurs. Footnote: (b) Hadrat Sha'bi said: 'Intention is in what is hidden; in any case, what is apparent has no intention in it.' (c) Hadrat Hasan said: 'A man who divorces his wife one divorce and intends three — he said: it is one.'
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. It states that when a husband uses explicit (sarih) divorce words, only one revocable divorce occurs, even if he intended more. The intention does not affect the number in sarih divorce. The author supports this with athar from al-Sha'bi and al-Hasan, which explicitly state that in explicit matters, intention is not considered, and that intending three does not make three. The footnotes reiterate these points. This establishes the Hanafi position that sarih divorce is independent of the husband's hidden intention regarding the count.

Key Terms
صريح (sarih): Explicit words of divorce that have no other meaning and require no intention.
كناية (kinayah): Ambiguous words that can imply divorce but require intention to be effective.

Explicit Divorce Does Not Require Intention
Ruling that explicit divorce words do not require intention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between sarih and kinayah divorce words and state the Hanafi ruling regarding intention for each.

Core Idea
Explicit divorce words (sarih) effect divorce immediately without the need for intention.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies a key Hanafi rule: explicit words of divorce, such as 'anti taliq,' are unambiguous and legally operative on their own. Unlike implicit (kinayah) expressions, which require intention or contextual evidence, sarih words do not depend on the husband's inner state. This ruling is based on the principle that apparent matters are judged by their outward form.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What are sarih divorce words? What is the ruling? The passage states that for explicit words, no intention is needed; divorce occurs upon utterance.
Sarih words are clear and unambiguous, e.g., 'You are divorced.'
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the statement of the Companions (as reported earlier) that intention is only considered in hidden matters; explicit words are apparent.
The dalil is a Qawl Sahabi, considered zanni in thubut and dalala.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Because the words are apparent (zahir), they are conclusive; intention is irrelevant. The ruling is derived from the principle that in manifest matters, the outward is taken as reality.
This is consistent with the Hanafi usul rule: al-amru al-zahiru la yahtaju ila niyyah.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) require intention even for explicit divorce words. They consider all divorce to be devotional acts requiring inner intent.
The Hanafi view distinguishes between sarih and kinayah.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi and is from Zahir al-Riwayah. The ruling provides legal certainty by not investigating subjective intent when words are clear.
The tarjih authority is the established Hanafi tradition; no specific later tarjih is mentioned in the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يفتقر بهذه الالفاظ الى نية يہ الفاظ نہیں محتاج ہیں نیت کے۔ ر صریح الفاظ میں طلاق دینے کے لئے نیت کی ضرورت نہیں ہے۔ بلکہ طلاق دیتے ہی بغیر نیت کے طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی۔
And these words are not in need of intention. These words do not require intention. Rather, in explicit words, there is no need for intention to give divorce. Rather, upon pronouncing divorce, divorce occurs without intention.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage states a clear ruling: explicit (sarih) divorce words do not require the husband's intention to take effect. The moment he utters the words, divorce occurs. The commentator emphasizes that unlike kinayah (implicit) words, sarih words are unambiguous and legally operative by themselves. The implicit proof is the same athar quoted earlier: the Companions' statements that intention is only relevant in hidden matters, not in apparent ones.

Key Terms
نية (niyyah): Intention in the heart; in divorce, it is necessary for kinayah but not for sarih.
صريح (sarih): Explicit, unambiguous divorce words.
كناية (kinayah): Implicit or indirect divorce words that require intention or contextual evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

Masdar Divorce Phrases: Intention and Number
Ruling on masdar-based divorce phrases and the role of intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on divorce using masdar phrases and apply the intention-based number determination for free and slave women.

Core Idea
Masdar divorce phrases count as one unless three intended; intention determines totality, not number.

This lesson examines a subtle aspect of Hanafi divorce law: the use of masdar-based phrases such as 'ant al-talaq'. These phrases, while explicit, have a unique linguistic feature that affects the number of divorces depending on the husband's intention. Understanding these rulings requires grasping two key principles of Arabic grammar as applied by the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the masdar phrases
Locate the phrases under discussion: 'ant al-talaq', 'anti talaq al-talaq', 'anti taliq talaq', and others. Note that they use the masdar (verbal noun) form.
The masdar is used in the sense of the active participle.
2
State the default ruling
If no intention is present, the default is one revocable divorce. The masdar indicates the minimum of the genus.
This is analogous to saying 'rajul 'adl' meaning 'a just man'.
3
Explain the role of intention
If the husband intends three divorces, three occur for a free woman, and two for a slave woman. Intention for a specific number like two is ineffective because masdar does not admit number.
Masdar indicates genus, not count; so only the totality (three for free, two for slave) can be intended.
4
Analyze the linguistic principles
First principle: masdar is used in the meaning of active participle, making the phrase sarih (explicit). Second principle: masdar does not admit number but admits genus.
This allows the husband to intend the entire genus of divorce for his wife's status.
5
Review the proofs
The passage cites the hadith of Ibn Umar (divorce during menses counts) and athar from Sha'bi and Hasan to support that explicit phrases do not require intention, but masdar forms do.
The hadith establishes the validity of explicit divorce; the athar clarify that intention in explicit phrases is irrelevant, but masdar forms are treated differently.

وقوله انت الطلاق وانت طالق الطلاق وانت طالق طلاقًا اور شوہر کا قول انت الطلاق ، انت طلاق الطلاق اور انت طالق طلاق میں اگر اس کی کوئی نیت نہیں ہے تو ایک رجعی طلاق ، اور دو کی نیت کی تو نہیں واقع ہوگی مگر ایک۔ اور اگر نیت کی اس سے تین کی تو تین ہوگی۔ طلاق اسم فاعل کے صیغے مثلا انت طالق سے واقع ہوتی ہے۔ اور چونکہ لفظ صریح ہے اس لئے ایک طلاق رجعی واقع ہوگی۔ لیکن حاشیہ : (الف) ابن عمر نے حیض کی حالت میں اپنی بیوی کو طلاق دی۔ پس حضرت عمر نے اس کا تذکر و حضور کے سامنے کیا تو آپ نے فرمایا اس کو رجعت کر لینا چاہئے ۔ میں نے کہا کہ کیا طلاق ہو جائے گی۔ آپ نے فرمایا تو پھر کیا ہو گا؟ (ب) حضرت شعمی نے فرمایا نیت اس چیز میں ہے جو پوشیدہ ہو۔ بہر حال جو ظا ہر ہو تو اس میں نیت نہیں ہے (ج) حضرت حسن نے فرمایا جو آدمی اپنی بیوی کو ایک طلاق دے اور تین کی نیت کرے۔ فرمایاوہ ایک ہی ہے۔ طالق طلاقًا فان لم تكن له نية فهى واحدة رجعية وان نوى ثنتين لا يقع الا واحدة وان نوى به ثلثا كان ثلثا صرف مصدر مثلا الطلاق استعمال کرے تو مصدر اسم فاعل کے معنی میں ہوتا ہے جیسے رجل عدل مصدر بول کر عادل مراد لیتے ہیں۔ اس لئے انت الطلاق مصدر بولا تو انت طالق اسم فاعل کے معنے میں ہو کر ایک طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی۔ مصدر کا دوسرا قاعدہ یہ ہے کہ وہ عدد کا احتمال نہیں رکھتا۔ اس لئے مصدر بول کر عدد کی نیت نہیں کر سکتے۔ اس لئے انت الطلاق بول کر دو طلاق کی نیت نہیں کر سکتے۔ البتہ جنس کا احتمال رکھتا ؟ ہے اس لئے مجموعے کی نیت کر سکتے ہیں۔ اس لئے نیت نہ ہو تو اقل درجہ ایک مراد ہو گی اور ایک طلاق واقع ہوگی۔ اور تین کی نیت کی ہو تو جنس کا اعتبار کرتے ہوئے مجموعہ واقع ہوگی۔ یعنی آزاد کو تین کیونکہ یہ اس کی آخری طلاق ہے۔ اور باندی کو دو کیونکہ یہی اس کی آخری طلاق ہے۔ سوال یہ مسائل اس اصول پر ہیں کہ مصدر اسم فاعل کے معنی میں ہوتا ہے۔ اور دوسرا اصول یہ ہے کہ مصدر عدد کا احتمال نہیں رکھتا، البتہ اسم جنس ہونے کی وجہ سے مجموعے کا احتمال رکھتا ہے۔ اس لئے تین واقع ہوگی دو طلاق نہیں۔ ات الطلاق : مصدر معرفہ ہے، طلاقا : مصدر نکرہ ہے۔
And his saying: 'You are the divorce' and 'You are divorced the divorce' and 'You are divorced a divorce' — and the husband's saying 'ant al-talaq', 'anti talaq al-talaq', and 'anti taliq talaq': if he has no intention, then one revocable divorce; and if he intends two, only one occurs; and if he intends three, three occur. Divorce occurs from the derivative of the active participle, e.g., 'anti taliq'. And since the word is explicit, one revocable divorce occurs. But footnote: (a) Ibn Umar gave his wife divorce while she was menstruating, so Umar mentioned it to the Prophet, and he said: 'He should take her back.' I said: 'Will it count?' He said: 'Then what?' (b) Hadrat Sha'bi said: 'Intention is in what is hidden; in any case, what is apparent has no intention in it.' (c) Hadrat Hasan said: 'A man who divorces his wife one divorce and intends three — he said: it is one.' (Continuing with Arabic): 'Divorced a divorce; if he has no intention, it is one revocable; if he intends two, only one occurs; if he intends three, it is three.' Then: 'Using the masdar, e.g., if he uses 'al-talaq', then the masdar is used in the meaning of the active participle, like 'rajul 'adl' using the masdar to mean 'adil'. Therefore, if he says 'ant al-talaq' using the masdar, it is in the sense of 'anti taliq', and one divorce occurs. The second rule of the masdar is that it does not admit number. Therefore, one cannot intend number by using the masdar. Thus, one cannot intend two divorces by saying 'ant al-talaq'. However, it admits genus, so one can intend the totality. Therefore, if there is no intention, the minimum (one) is meant, and one divorce occurs. And if he intends three, then considering the genus, the totality occurs, i.e., for a free woman, three (since that is her final divorce); for a slave woman, two (since that is her final divorce). So the question is: these issues are based on the principle that the masdar is in the meaning of the active participle, and the second principle is that the masdar does not admit number, but as a noun of genus, it admits totality. Therefore, three occur, not two. 'at-talaq' is a definite masdar; 'talaqan' is an indefinite masdar.'
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed ruling on divorce using masdar forms such as 'ant al-talaq'. The key points: these forms are considered sarih (explicit) because the masdar is used in the sense of the active participle. The number of divorces depends on intention: if no intention, one revocable divorce; if intention for three, three occur (for a free woman) or two (for a slave woman). The reasoning uses two linguistic principles: (1) masdar can mean the active participle; (2) masdar does not admit number but can indicate genus/totality. Thus, intending two is not possible, but intending three is possible because three is the entire genus. The passage also includes proof from the hadith of Ibn Umar and athar from Shabi and Hasan, and special rules for slave women.

Key Terms
مصدر (masdar): Verbal noun; in this context, it is used in the sense of the active participle to effect divorce.

Key Takeaway
Masdar divorce phrases like 'ant al-talaq' are explicit in Hanafi fiqh; without intention one revocable divorce occurs, and with intention for three, three occur for a free wife.

Kinayah Divorce: The Three Words and the Need for Intention
Introduces kinayah divorce and three specific words giving one revocable divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three kinayah words that yield one revocable divorce and explain why intention (niyyah) or contextual indication (dalalah al-hal) is required, citing the hadith of Rukanah.

Core Idea
In kinayah divorce, intention is the key — without it, the word is ambiguous and no divorce occurs.

You already learned about sarih (explicit) words of divorce. Now we turn to kinayah (implicit) words — those that have more than one meaning. This passage introduces the general rule that kinayah divorce requires intention or contextual indication, and then highlights three specific words that always result in one revocable divorce. The proof is the famous hadith of Rukanah, which the author uses to show that the Prophet (peace be upon him) asked about Rukanah's intention before ruling on the divorce.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والضرب الثانى الكنايات ولا يقع بها الطلاق الا بالنية او بدلالة حال وهى على ضربين منها ثلثة الفاظ يقع بها الطلاق الرجعي ولا يقع بها الا واحدة وهي اعتدي واستبرئي رحمك وانت واحدة پہلے گزر چکا ہے کہ طلاق کنایہ ان الفاظ سے ہوتی ہے جن کے دو معنی ہوں۔ ایک معنی کے اعتبار سے طلاق ہوتی ہو اور دوسرے معنی کے اعتبار سے واقع نہیں ہوتی ہو۔ چونکہ ان کے دو معنی ہیں اس لئے یا تو نیت کرے یا حالات کا تقاضہ ہو کہ وہ طلاق ہی دینا چاہتا ہے تب طلاق واقع ہوگی۔ حدیث میں اس کا ثبوت ہے۔ عن عبد الله بن على بن يزيد بن ركانة عن ابيه عن جده انه طلق امرأته البتة فاتي رسول الله إلى الله فقال ما اردت ؟ قال واحدة قال الله ؟ قال الله قال هو على ما اردت (الف) (ابوداؤد شریف، باب فی الباب ٣٠٧ نمبر ٢٢٠٨ / ترمذی شریف ، باب ما جاء فی الرجل يطلق امرأته البته ص ٢٢٢ نمبر ١١٧٧) اس حدیث میں حضرت رکانہ نے طلاق البتہ دیا ہے جس کے دو معنی ہیں (١) کسی چیز کو کاٹنا (٢) اور نکاح کو کاٹنا۔ چونکہ دو معنی تھے اس لئے حضور نے رکانہ سے پوچھا کیا نیت کی ہے؟ انہوں نے فرمایا ایک طلاق کی۔ پس آپ نے فرمایا جتنی نیت کی ہے اتنی ہی طلاق واقع ہوگی۔ اس سے معلوم ہوا الفاظ کنایہ میں طلاق واقع ہونے کے لئے نیت کی ضرورت ہوگی۔ اس عبارت میں دوسرا مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ الفاظ کنایات میں سے تین الفاظ ایسے ہیں جن سے ایک طلاق رجعی واقع ہوگی۔ حاشیہ : (الف) حضرت رکانہ نے اپنی بیوی کو طلاق البتہ دی اور حضور کے پاس آئے اور کہا میں نے نہیں ارادہ کیا ہے مگر ایک ۔ آپ نے فرمایا خدا کی قسم ایک ہی کی نیت کی ہے؟ حضرت رکانہ نے فرمایا خدا کی قسم۔ آپ نے فرمایا جو ارادہ کیا وہی صحیح ہے۔
And the second type is the kinayat; divorce does not occur with them except with intention or contextual indication. And they are of two types: among them are three words by which revocable divorce occurs, and only one occurs: they are 'complete your waiting period', 'clear your womb', and 'you are one'. It has already passed that kinayah divorce is from words that have two meanings: in one sense divorce occurs, and in the other sense it does not. Since they have two meanings, either he must intend or the situation demands that he wants divorce; then divorce occurs. There is proof of this in a hadith: from Abdullah ibn Ali ibn Yazid ibn Rukanah from his father from his grandfather that he divorced his wife 'al-battah' and came to the Messenger of Allah and said: 'I intended only one.' He said: 'By Allah, did you intend one?' He said: 'By Allah.' He said: 'It is as you intended.' (Abu Dawud, chapter on al-batt, no. 2208; Tirmidhi, chapter on a man divorcing his wife al-battah, no. 1177). In this hadith, Hadrat Rukanah gave divorce 'al-battah', which has two meanings: (1) cutting something, (2) cutting the marriage. Since it had two meanings, the Prophet asked him what he intended. He said one divorce. Then he said: 'As you intended, so it is.' From this it is understood that in kinayah words, intention is necessary for divorce to occur. In this passage, the second issue is that among the kinayah words, three are such that one revocable divorce occurs from them. Footnote: (a) Hadrat Rukanah gave his wife 'al-battah' divorce and came to the Prophet and said: 'I intended only one.' He said: 'By Allah, did you intend only one?' He said: 'By Allah.' He said: 'What you intended is correct.'
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage defines the second category of divorce: kinayah (implicit) words. Unlike sarih, kinayah words require either the husband's intention or a contextual indication that he intends divorce. The passage further states that among kinayah words, three specific words yield one revocable divorce: 'I'taddi' (complete your waiting period), 'Istibri'i rahimak' (clear your womb), and 'Anti wahida' (you are one). The proof is the famous hadith of Rukanah, who used the word 'al-battah' (which can mean 'cutting' or 'absolute divorce') and the Prophet verified his intention. This establishes that intention is crucial for kinayah divorce.

Key Terms
كنایہ (kinayah): Implicit words of divorce that have more than one meaning; divorce only occurs with intention or contextual indication.
البتة (al-battah): An ambiguous word meaning 'cutting' or 'absolute'; used for divorce by Rukanah.
اعتدى (i'taddi): 'Complete your waiting period' — one of the three kinayah words that yield one revocable divorce without intention.
استبرئي رحمك (istibri'i rahimak): 'Clear your womb' — one of the three kinayah words that yield one revocable divorce without intention.
انت واحدة (anti wahida): 'You are one' — one of the three kinayah words that yield one revocable divorce without intention.

Three kinayah words — 'I'taddi', 'Istibri'i rahimak', and 'Anti wahida' — effect one revocable divorce without requiring intention, while other kinayah words require intention or contextual indication.

Talaq — Divorce

6 min
Three Kinayah Words Yielding One Revocable Divorce
Ruling on three specific kinayah words for divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the three kinayah words (i'taddi, istabri'i rahimik, anti wahidah) and explain why each yields one revocable divorce.

Core Idea
These three kinayah words imply the explicit phrase 'anti taliq', thus one revocable divorce occurs.

In this lesson, we examine a specific subset of kinayah words in Hanafi law: 'i'taddi', 'istabri'i rahimik', and 'anti wahidah'. These words do not directly say 'divorce', but the well of jurisprudence (wajh) reveals they necessarily imply the explicit phrase 'anti taliq'. Consequently, when uttered with the intention of divorce, they effect one revocable divorce (talaq raj'i). We will trace the legal reasoning and the prophetic proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three kinayah words
The author lists three specific kinayah words: 'i'taddi' (observe your waiting period), 'istabri'i rahimik' (purify your womb), and 'anti wahidah' (you are one). These are the focus of the lesson.
They are a subset of all kinayah words for divorce.
2
Understand the concept of kinayah
Kinayah words are non-explicit expressions that require the husband's intention to effect divorce. The Hanafi school treats them as valid, with the missing explicit phrase 'anti taliq' understood.
This distinguishes them from sarih (explicit) words, which need no intention.
3
Analyze the implied explicit phrase for each word
For example, 'i'taddi' implies 'anti taliq fa'taddi' (you are divorced, so observe the waiting period). Similarly, 'istabri'i rahimik' implies 'anti taliq fa'stabri'i rahimik', and 'anti wahidah' implies 'anti taliq tatliqatan wahidah'.
The explicit phrase determines the ruling.
4
Apply the hadith evidence
The Prophet ﷺ said to Sawdah bint Zam'ah 'i'taddi' and counted it as one revocable divorce (huwa amlak biha). This is the primary proof that such kinayah words produce a revocable divorce.
The hadith is from Sunan al-Bayhaqi and is accepted in Hanafi law.
5
Conclude the ruling and limitations
Since the explicit phrase yields one revocable divorce, these kinayah words also yield one revocable divorce. For 'anti wahidah', the number is explicitly limited to one, so the husband cannot intend two or three.
The revocability means the husband may take back his wife during the waiting period.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحدة وهي قوله اعتدی و استبرئ رحمك وانت واحد [۱۹۰۷] (٢٣) وبقية الكنايات وجہ یہ ہے کہ ان الفاظ میں انت طالق لفظ صریح محذوف ماننا پڑے گا۔ اور انت طالق سے ایک طلاق رجعی واقع ہوتی ہے اس لئے ان الفاظ سے بھی ایک طلاق رجعی واقع ہوگی۔ مثلا اعتدی کا ترجمہ ہے عدت گزار لو تو اس کا مطلب یہ ہوا کہ اس سے پہلے میں نے طلاق دے دی ہے اس لئے عدت گزار لو۔ عبارت یوں ہوگی انت طالق فاعتدی ۔ اس لئے اعتدی کے لفظ کنایہ ہے(۲) حدیث میں ہے۔ عن ابی هريرة أن رسول الله قال لسودة بنت زمعه اعتدى فجعلها تطليقة واحدة وهو املك بها (الف) (سن ليبقى ، باب ما جاء في كنايات الطلاق التي لا يقع الطلاق بھا الا ان برید مخرج الكلام منه الطلاق ج سابع ، ص ۵۶۱، نمبر ۱۵۰۰۶ر مصنف ابن ابی شیبه ۲۴ فی الرجل يقول لامرأته اعتدی ما یکون ج رابع ہیں، ۷۲ ، نمبر (۱۷۸۹) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ اعتدی سے ایک طلاق شمار کی اور ھو املک بھا سے رجعی کا پتہ چلا۔ اور استبرنی رحمک کا ترجمہ ہے اپنا ر تم صاف کر لو۔ اس کے دو مطلب ہیں۔ رحم صاف کر لو اس لئے کہ مجھے جماع کرتا ہے۔ اور دوسرا ترجمہ ہے رحم صاف کر لو اس لئے کہ میں نے تم کو طلاق دے دی ہے۔ اور عبارت یوں ہوگی انــت طــالـق فاستبرنی رحمک ۔ اور انت طالق سے ایک طلاق رجعی واقع ہوتی ہے۔ اس لئے استبرئی رحمک سے ایک طلاق رجعی واقع ہوگی۔ اور انت واحدہ کا ترجمہ ہے تو اکیلی ہے۔ یا تجھ کو ایک طلاق ہے۔ اور عبارت ہوگی انت طالق تطليقة واحدة ۔ اس لئے صریح لفظ سے ایک طلاق رجعی واقع ہوگی ۔ چونکہ واحدۃ کا لفظ موجود ہے اس لئے دو اور تین کی بھی نیت نہیں کر سکتا۔
One is his saying 'i'taddi' (observe your waiting period), 'istabri'i rahimik' (purify your womb), and 'anti wahidah' (you are one). [1907] (23) And the remaining kinayah words: the reason is that in these words we must assume the explicit word 'anti taliq' (you are divorced) to be omitted. And from 'anti taliq' one revocable divorce occurs, so from these words also one revocable divorce occurs. For example, 'i'taddi' means 'observe your waiting period', so its meaning is that before this I have divorced you, therefore observe the waiting period. The expression would be 'anti taliq fa'taddi' (you are divorced, so observe the waiting period). Therefore the word 'i'taddi' is a kinayah. (2) There is a hadith: from Abu Hurairah that the Messenger of Allah ﷺ said to Sawdah bint Zam'ah 'i'taddi', and he made it one divorce, and he had the right to take her back. (Reference: Sunan al-Bayhaqi, Bab ma ja'a fi kinayat al-talaq...). This hadith shows that one divorce was counted from 'i'taddi', and 'huwa amlak biha' indicates revocability. And 'istabri'i rahimik' means 'purify your womb'. It has two meanings: purify your womb because I want to have intercourse with you, and the second meaning is purify your womb because I have divorced you. The expression would be 'anti taliq fa'stabri'i rahimik'. And from 'anti taliq' one revocable divorce occurs, so from 'istabri'i rahimik' one revocable divorce occurs. And 'anti wahidah' means 'you are alone' or 'you have one divorce'. The expression would be 'anti taliq tatliqatan wahidah' (you are divorced one divorce). Therefore from the explicit word one revocable divorce occurs. Since the word 'wahidah' is present, he cannot intend two or three.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling on three specific kinayah (non-explicit) words for divorce: i'taddi (observe your waiting period), istabri'i rahimik (purify your womb), and anti wahidah (you are one). The author classifies these as kinayah because they do not directly state divorce, but they imply it. The legal reasoning (wajh) is that in these expressions we must assume the explicit phrase 'anti taliq' (you are divorced) is intended but omitted. Since the explicit phrase would effect one revocable divorce (talaq raj'i), these kinayah words also produce one revocable divorce. The author provides evidence from a hadith in which the Prophet ﷺ said to Sawdah bint Zam'ah 'i'taddi', and this was considered one revocable divorce because the Prophet ﷺ had the right to take her back (huwa amlak biha). Similarly, for istabri'i rahimik, the implied meaning is that divorce has occurred, so she must purify her womb after the divorce. For anti wahidah, the explicit number restricts the divorce to one, and since the word 'wahidah' is stated, the husband cannot intend two or three divorces. Thus, these three words are treated as kinayah but with the effect of explicit wording, giving one revocable divorce.

Key Terms
کنایہ (kinayah): A word that implies divorce without explicitly stating it, requiring intention for effect.
طلاق رجعی (talaq raj'i): A revocable divorce where the husband can take back his wife during the waiting period without a new marriage contract.
صریح (sarih): Explicit words of divorce that effect divorce without need for intention (e.g., 'anti taliq').

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) 'I'taddi' (observe your waiting period)
B) 'Anti ba'in' (you are irrevocably divorced)
C) 'Anti haram' (you are forbidden)
D) 'Anti khaliyah' (you are free)

Key Takeaway
Words like 'i'taddi', 'istabri'i rahimik', and 'anti wahidah' are kinayah that, when intended for divorce, effect one revocable divorce.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
8 min
Rulings on Remaining Kinayah Words for Divorce
Rulings on intention and effect for all other kinayah words

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for kinayah words: one irrevocable divorce if one intended, one if two intended, three if three intended, and explain the principle of jins with evidence.

Core Idea
For non-explicit divorce words, intention decides the outcome: one if one intended, three if three, but two counts as one.

The previous lesson covered three specific kinayah words ('i'taddi', 'istabri'i rahimik', 'anti wahidah'). Now we turn to all other kinayah words. The core rule is simple: without intention, no divorce; with intention, the number depends on what the husband intends. However, if he intends two divorces, only one occurs—a unique Hanafi position rooted in the linguistic nature of these words as indicating a genus.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule
For all kinayah words besides the three previously mentioned, if the husband has no intention, no divorce occurs. If he intends divorce, one irrevocable divorce occurs.
Intention is the decisive factor.
2
Analyze the number based on intention
If he intends one divorce → one talaq ba'in. If he intends two → still one talaq ba'in. If he intends three → three divorces.
Two is not a valid intention because these words signify the genus (jins) of divorce, and the totality is three.
3
Examine the evidence for one irrevocable divorce
The author cites an athar from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i on 'la sabila li alayki' and the hadith of Ibnat al-Jawn where the Prophet said 'alhaqi bi-ahlik' intending divorce. Both show that one irrevocable divorce occurs with general intention.
These texts establish the ba'in nature, not raj'i.
4
Understand the logic for two counting as one
Kinayah words imply the genus (jins) of divorce. The total number of the genus is three. Two is neither an individual nor the total, so it defaults to the minimum of one.
This is derived from usul: kinayah carries jins, not 'adad.
5
Review the proof for three divorces
The athar from Ali (ibn Abi Talib) states that specific kinayah words equal three if intended. The hadith of Rifa'ah (in Bukhari) also shows that 'batt' (a kinayah word) was understood as three divorces.
Thus, if the husband intends three, three occur.

And the remaining kinayah words: if he intends divorce, one irrevocable divorce occurs. And if he intends three, then three, and if he intends two, then one. Aside from the above three words, all kinayah words: if he does not intend, nothing occurs. And if he intends divorce, one irrevocable divorce occurs, not revocable. And if he intends two divorces, still only one divorce occurs, not two. And if he intends three divorces, three divorces occur. The reason that one irrevocable divorce occurs upon intention is this athar: from Ibrahim (al-Nakha'i) who said: if a man says 'la sabila li alayki' (I have no way over you), it is one irrevocable divorce. (Reference: Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah, Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq). In this athar, 'la sabila li alayki' is a kinayah word, and with the intention of divorce, Ibrahim ruled one irrevocable divorce. So it is known that upon intention, one irrevocable divorce occurs. (2) There is a hadith: the Prophet ﷺ said to 'Amrah bint al-Jawn 'alhaqi bi-ahlik' (join your family) and he intended divorce, so divorce occurred. From Aisha: when Ibnat al-Jawn was brought to the Prophet ﷺ and he came near her, she said 'A'udhu billahi minka', so he said to her 'Laqad 'udhti bi'azim, alhaqi bi-ahlik.' (Reference: Bukhari, Bab man tallaqa...). When he intended divorce by it, it was one irrevocable divorce; if he intended three, it was three; if he intended two, it was one. [1908] (24) And these are like his saying 'anti ba'in', 'battah', 'batlah', 'haram', 'habluki 'ala gharibik', 'alhaqi bi-ahlik', 'khaliyyah', 'bariyyah', 'wahbatu li-ahlik', 'asrahtuki', 'ikhtari', 'faraqtuki', 'anti...' (facing the man his wife with divorce). And the reason that intention of two divorces does not produce two: it has passed earlier that these words carry the probability of jins (genus), from which totality is intended, and the totality of divorce is three; so if he intends three, three occur, and two is neither an individual nor a totality. Therefore if he intends two, two do not occur. For the intention of three occurring from kinayah words, there is this athar: from Ali (رضي الله عنه) who said: 'al-khaliyyah, al-bariyyah, al-battah, al-ba'in, al-haram' when he intends, it is in the position of three. (Reference: Sunan al-Bayhaqi). (2) Proof that uttering a kinayah word with intention of three produces three divorces is the long hadith of Rifa'ah, where it says 'tallaqani fatt batta talāqī' (he divorced me and made my divorce final). The segment of the hadith: that Rifa'ah divorced me and made my divorce final, and then I married Abd al-Rahman ibn al-Zubayr al-Qurazi. (Reference: Bukhari). This hadith shows that a kinayah word can be used to intend three divorces. (3) From Ibrahim concerning 'al-khaliyyah': if he intends divorce, then the minimum is one irrevocable divorce; if the husband and wife wish, he can remarry her; and if he intends three, then three. (Reference: Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah). [1908] (24) And these are the kinayah words: (1) you are separated from me, (2) your connection with me is cut, (3) you are forbidden, (4) your rope is on your neck, (5) join your family, (6) you are absolutely left, (7) you are absolutely free, (8) I have given you to your relatives, (9) I have released you, (10) choose for yourself, (11) I have parted from you, (12) you are free, (13) wrap yourself in a shawl, (14) observe purdah, (15) go away, (16) seek a husband. All these words are kinayah: they have two meanings. With the intention of one meaning, divorce occurs; without intention, no divorce occurs. The proof for some words has come in the hadiths above, e.g., al-battah, khaliyyah, bariyyah, haram, and alhaqi bi-ahlik. The rest can be analogized to these. There is also another athar: that a man said to his wife 'habluki 'ala gharibik' (your rope is on your neck), and he said it repeatedly. (Footnote from previous page continued...)

Commentary
This passage explains the rulings for all kinayah words for divorce except the three (i'taddi, istabri'i rahimik, anti wahidah) which were already covered. For all other kinayah words, the key factor is the husband's intention (niyyah). If he utters these words without any intention of divorce, no divorce occurs. If he intends divorce, one irrevocable divorce (talaq ba'in) occurs. If he intends three divorces, three occur. If he intends two, only one occurs because these words are considered to indicate the genus (jins) of divorce, and the totality of the genus is three; two is not a valid intention as it is neither a single nor a triple. The author provides evidence from athar (reports of the Companions and Successors) and hadith. For example, the athar of Ibrahim al-Nakha'i on 'la sabila li alayki' (no way over you) shows it gives one irrevocable divorce. The hadith of 'Amrah bint al-Jawn where the Prophet said 'alhaqi bi-ahlik' (join your family) shows that intention of divorce makes it one irrevocable, intention of three makes it three, and intention of two makes it one. The author also lists a variety of common kinayah words such as 'anti ba'in' (you are irrevocably separate), 'anti battah' (you are final), 'anti haram' (you are forbidden), 'habluki 'ala gharibik' (your rope is on your neck), 'alhaqi bi-ahlik' (join your family), 'khaliyyah' (free), 'bariyyah' (free), and others. All these follow the same rule: they require intention, and the number of divorces depends on what the husband intends, with the limit that two cannot be intended as a separate number. The hadith of Rifa'ah (the woman who said 'tallaqani fatt batta talāqī') is used to prove that three divorces can be effected by kinayah words. The athar of Ali ibn Abi Talib states that specific kinayah words amount to three if intended. The athar of Ibrahim al-Nakha'i on 'khaliyyah' confirms that the minimum is one irrevocable divorce and three if so intended. The passage concludes by stating that all these words have two possible meanings, and it is intention that distinguishes divorce from other meanings.

Key Terms
کنایہ (kinayah): Non-explicit words that may imply divorce but require intention for effect.
طلاق بائن (talaq ba'in): An irrevocable divorce that ends the marriage immediately; the husband cannot take back his wife during the waiting period without a new marriage contract.
نیّت (niyyah): Intention in the heart; for kinayah words, the husband's intention determines whether divorce occurs and how many.
جنس (jins): Genus; in this context, the general category of divorce. Kinayah words indicate the genus, not a specific number.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says to his wife 'anti khaliyyah' and intends two divorces, how many divorces occur according to the text?
A) Two divorces
B) Three divorces
C) One irrevocable divorce
D) No divorce

Key Takeaway
The number of divorces from kinayah words depends on the husband's intention: one for intention of one or two, three for intention of three; no divorce without intention.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate

Intention with Kinayah Words of Divorce
Ruling on kinayah words: intention determines number of divorces

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on kinayah divorce words and apply the principle that intention determines the number of divorces (one bain or three).

Core Idea
With kinayah, intention is decisive: one intended = one bain; three intended = three; two intended = one bain.

Divorce (talaq) can be effected through explicit words (sarih) or indirect expressions (kinayah). The Hanafi school gives central weight to the husband's intention when kinayah words are used. In this passage, the commentator establishes the core rule and supports it with evidence from the Companions and the Prophet (peace be upon him).

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on kinayah
State the effect of intention: if the husband intends divorce, one bain occurs; if he intends three, three occur; if he intends two, still one bain because two is neither a single nor a total.
2
Recognize the condition for kinayah
Kinayah words have two meanings (divorce and non-divorce). Intention is required to distinguish which meaning is intended.
3
Examine the proofs
Three proofs are cited: (1) athar of Ali stating that kinayah words are equivalent to three when intended; (2) hadith of Rifaa (Bukhari) where the word 'batt' was used and understood as three without objection; (3) athar of Ibrahim that with 'khaliyyah' intention of divorce gives one bain, intention of three gives three.
4
Understand the exception for intending two
Intending two divorces does not result in two; it results in one bain because the kinayah words denote a genus (divorce as a category), and two is neither a single nor the total (three).
5
Apply qiyas to other kinayah words
The commentator lists many kinayah words (e.g., 'bain', 'batta', 'khaliyyah') and notes that the ruling extends to all by analogy (qiyas) based on the words that have direct textual evidence.
