اذا نوى بها الطلاق كانت واحدة بائنة وان نوى ثلثا كانت ثلثا وان نوى ثنتين كانت واحدة [ ۱۹۰۸] (۲۴) وهذه مثل قوله انت بائن وبتة وبتلة وحرام و حبلک علی غاربک والحقى باهلک و خليَّة و بريَّة ووهبتک لاهلک اسرحتک و اختاری و فارقتك وانت يواجه الرجل امرأته بالطلاق ص ۷۹۰ نمبر ۵۲۵۴) اور دو طلاق کی نیت کرے گا تو دو واقع نہیں ہوگی اس کی وجہ پہلے گزر چکی ہے کہ یہ الفاظ جنس کا احتمال رکھتے ہیں جس سے مجموعہ مراد ہوگی اور طلاق کا مجموعہ تین ہے اس لئے تین کی نیت کرے گا تو تین واقع ہوگی اور دو نہ فرداقل ہے اور نہ مجموعہ ہے۔ اس لئے دو کی نیت کرے گا تو دو واقع نہیں ہوگی ۔ الفاظ کنایات میں تین کی نیت کرے گا تو تین واقع ہوگی اس کے لئے یہ اثر ہے۔ عـن عـلـى قال الخلية والبرية والبتة والبائن والحرام اذا نواى فهو بمنزلة الثلاث (الف) (سن للبیہقی ، باب قال في الکنایات انھا ثلث ج سابع ص ۵۶۴، نمبر ۱۵۰۱۷ (۲) لفظ کنایہ بول کر تین کی نیت کرنے سے تین طلاق واقع ہوگی اس کی دلیل حضرت رفاعہ کی لمبی حدیث ہے جس میں طلقنی ثبت طلاقی کہا ہے اور بہت لفظ کنایہ سے تین طلاق مراد لی ہے۔ حدیث کا ٹکڑا یہ ہے۔ ان رفاعة طلقني فبت طلاقی وانی نکحت بعده عبد الرحمن بن زبیر القرظی (ب) ( بخاری شریف، باب من جوز الطلاق الثلاث ص ۷۹۱ نمبر ۵۲۶۰) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ لفظ کنایہ بول کر تین طلاق مراد لی جاسکتی ہے۔ (۳) عن ابراهيم في الخلية ان نوی طلاقـا فـادنـي مـا يكون تطليقه بائن ان شاء وشاءت تزوجها وان نوى ثلاثا فثلاث ( ج ) ( مصنف ابن ابی شیبۃ ۶۴ ما قالوا في الخلية ج رابع ، ص ۵۶، نمبر ۱۸۱۴۸) اس اثر میں ہے کہ طلاق کی نیت کرے گا تو ادنی درجہ ایک طلاق بائنہ واقع ہوگی اور تین کی نیست کریا گا تو تین طلاقیں واقع ہوں گی۔ [ ۱۹۰۸ (۲۴) اور وہ الفاظ کنایہ ہیں (۱) تو مجھ سے جدا ہو جا (۳/۲) تیرا مجھ سے قطع تعلق ہے (۴) تو حرام ہے (۵) تیری رسی تیری گردن پر ہے (۶) تو اپنے اہل کے ساتھ مل جا (۷) تو بالکل چھوڑ دی گئی (۸) تو بالکل بری ہے (۹) تجھے تیرے عزیزوں کو ہبہ کر دیا (۱۰) میں نے تجھے چھوڑ دیا (۱۱) خود مختار ہو جا (۱۲) میں نے تجھے جدا کر دیا (۱۳) تو آزاد ہے (۱۴) چادر اوڑھ لے (۱۵) پردہ کرلے (۱۶) دور ہو جا (۱۷) شوہروں کو تلاش کر لے۔
ا یہ سب الفاظ کنایہ کے ہیں جن کے دو معنی ہیں۔ ایک معنی کی نیت سے طلاق واقع ہوگی اور کچھ نیت نہ کرنے سے طلاق واقع نہیں ہوگی۔ کچھ الفاظ کی دلیل او پر احادیث میں آچکی ہے۔ مثلا البتة، خلية ، برية ، حرام اور الحقی با ھلک سے طلاق ہونے کی دلیل اوپر گز بر چکی ہے۔ اس پر باقی کو قیاس کر لیں۔ ایک اثر یہ بھی ہے۔ ان رجلا قال لامرأته حبلک علی غاربک قال ذلک مرارا حاشیہ : ( پچھلے صفحہ سے آگے ) آپ اس سے قریب ہوئے تو بنت جون نے کہا اعوذ باللہ منک، تو آپ نے اس سے فرمایا بڑی ذات سے پناہ مانگی ہے اپنے اہل کے پاس چلی جاؤ (الف) حضرت علی نے فرمایا خلیہ، بریتہ بستہ ، بائن اور حرام سے اگر نیت کرے تو وہ تین طلاقوں کے درجے میں ہیں (ب ) حضرت رفاعہ نے مجھے طلاق دی تو تین طلاقیں دی اور میں نے اس کے بعد عبد الرحمن بن زبیر قرظی سے شادی کی (ج) حضرت ابراہیم نے خلیہ کے باری میں کہا اگر طلاق کی نیت کی تو کم سے کم ایک طلاق بائنہ واقع ہوگی۔ اگر شوہر اور اس کی بیوی چاہے۔ اور اگر تین کی نیت کرے تو تین واقع ہوگی۔
حرة وتقنعى و استترى واغربى وابتغى الازواج
If he intends divorce with it, it is one bain; if he intends three, it is three; if he intends two, it is one. [1908](24) And these are like his statement: you are bain, and batta, and battala, and haram, and your rope is on your hump, and join your family, and khaliyyah, and bariyyah, and I have gifted you to your family, I have released you, and choose, and I have separated from you, and you face the man his wife with divorce. (p.790 no.5254) And if he intends two divorces, they will not occur because these words imply the genus from which the total is intended, and the total of divorce is three. So if he intends three, they occur; and two is neither an individual nor a total, so if he intends two, they do not occur. In kinayah words, if he intends three, they occur. For this there is this athar: from Ali: he said, 'al-khaliyyah, al-bariyyah, al-battah, al-bain, and al-haram — if he intends, it is in the position of three.' (Sunan al-Bayhaqi, bab qal fi al-kinayat annaha thalith, j 7 p.564 no.15017) (2) Using a kinayah word and intending three divorces, they occur. The evidence is the long hadith of Rifaa in which he said 'He divorced me giving three divorces...' A portion of the hadith: 'Rifaa divorced me and gave me three divorces, and after him I married Abd al-Rahman ibn Zubayr al-Qurazi.' (Bukhari, bab man jawwaz al-talaq al-thalath, p.791 no.5260) From this hadith it is known that by a kinayah word three divorces can be intended. (3) From Ibrahim regarding al-khaliyyah: if he intends divorce, then the least that occurs is one bain divorce; he and she may remarry; and if he intends three, then three. (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba, ma qalu fi al-khaliyyah, j 4 p.56 no.18148) In this athar it is stated that if he intends divorce, the minimum is one bain; and if he intends three, three occur. [1908](24) And those are the kinayah words: (1) separate from me, (2/3) your connection with me is cut, (4) you are haram, (5) your rope is on your neck, (6) join your people, (7) you are completely freed, (8) you are completely released, (9) I have gifted you to your relatives, (10) I have left you, (11) become independent, (12) I have separated you, (13) you are free, (14) put on a cloak, (15) veil yourself, (16) go away, (17) seek husbands. All these are kinayah words having two meanings. With one meaning intention divorce occurs; without intention it does not occur. The evidence for some words has come in hadith, e.g., al-battah, khaliyyah, bariyyah, haram, and al-haqi bi-ahlik. The rest are measured by qiyas. There is also this athar: a man said to his wife 'your rope is on your hump' and said it repeatedly. (Footnote continuation from previous page) He approached her, and Bint al-Jawn said 'I seek refuge with Allah from you.' So he said to her: 'You have sought refuge with the Great; go to your family.' (a) Ali said: khaliyyah, bariyyah, battah, bain, and haram — if he intends, they are in the degree of three. (b) Rifaa gave me three divorces. (c) Ibrahim said regarding khaliyyah: if he intends divorce, the least is one bain; if the husband and wife wish; and if he intends three, they occur. Hurrah, and taqanna'i, and istatiri, and ighribi, and ibtaghi al-azwaj.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This is a commentary on the ruling of kinayah words in divorce. The passage begins by stating the core ruling: when a husband uses a kinayah word (indirect expression) to divorce his wife, the number of divorces depends on his intention. If he intends divorce in general, it counts as one bain divorce. If he intends three, it counts as three. If he intends two, it still counts as one bain because two is neither an individual nor a total; the words imply a single act of divorce as a genus. The author then lists many kinayah words, such as 'bain', 'batta', 'khaliyyah', etc. The commentary explains that these words have two possible meanings: divorce or something else, so intention is essential. The author provides proofs: the athar from Ali that these words are equivalent to three when intended; the hadith of Rifaa where the wife says he divorced her with three (using the word 'batt' which is kinayah); and the athar from Ibrahim that with 'khaliyyah', the minimum is one bain. The passage then lists seventeen specific kinayah words and explains their usage. Finally, it notes that some words have evidence from hadith (e.g., it is established that 'al-haqi bi-ahlik' caused divorce in the incident of Bint al-Jawn), and other words are analogically extended. All examples are from Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
کناية (kinayah): Words that are not explicitly used for divorce but imply it, requiring intention for divorce to occur.
بائن (ba'in): A divorce that results in complete separation, allowing remarriage only with a new contract.
بتة (battah): A kinayah word meaning 'cut off completely', often used for divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband says to his wife 'khaliyyah' (you are freed) intending to give three divorces. According to the Hanafi ruling, what occurs?
A) One bain divorce occurs.
B) Three bain divorces occur.
C) One raj'i divorce occurs.
D) No divorce occurs.

Key Takeaway
With kinayah words, the husband's intention is decisive: one intended = one bain; three intended = three; two intended = one bain.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
8 min
Kinayah Divorce: Intention and Context
Ruling when no intention: no divorce unless in a divorce discussion context

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for kinayah divorce when intention is absent, and distinguish between judicial (qada) and divine accountability.

Core Idea
No divorce by kinayah without intention — except context may infer it for the court, not for Allah.

When a husband uses a kinayah word like 'Go to your family' without intending divorce, does it count? Hanafis say no — unless the words are spoken during a discussion about divorce. Even then, the court may rule a divorce, but between the husband and Allah, only his true intention matters. This lesson unpacks the proofs and the crucial distinction between judicial and divine accountability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and Its Exception
Read the first sentence: if no intention, no divorce. Then note the exception: unless they are in a divorce discussion (muthakarah al-talaq), then divorce occurs in the judicial ruling (qada).
The exception applies only to judicial ruling, not necessarily before Allah.
2
Examine the Proofs
Three proofs: (1) the incident of a man who said 'habluk ala gharik' and Umar made him swear about his intention; (2) the hadith of Aisha that the Prophet gave his wives choice, and no divorce occurred because they did not intend it; (3) the hadith of Bint al-Jawn where the Prophet said 'al-haqi bi-ahlik' in a divorce context and it was considered a divorce.
Each proof shows the role of intention or context.
3
Understand the Qada vs. Baynahu wa Bayna Allah Distinction
The passage emphasizes that in a divorce discussion context, the court (qada) will rule divorce occurred, but between him and Allah, it only counts if he intended. This is a key Hanafi principle: external circumstances can trigger a judicial ruling, but the inner state determines the divine ruling.
This protects a husband from unintended consequences while maintaining social order.
4
Apply to a Scenario
Consider: During a heated argument about divorce, a husband says 'Your rope is on your shoulder' but has no inner intention. According to the Hanafi ruling, the court would declare a divorce, but before Allah, no divorce occurred. If he later denies intention, the court accepts his oath unless evidence shows otherwise.
Practice distinguishing between the two realms.

حاشیہ : (الف) ایک آدمی نے اپنی بیوی سے تیری رسی تیرے کندھے پر ہے کہا اورکئی مرتبہ کہا۔ پھر عمر بن خطاب کے پاس آئے ۔ انہوں نے اس کو رکن اور مقام ابراہیم کے درمیان قسم دی کہ اپنی بات سے کیا ارادہ کیا ؟ آدمی نے کہا میں نے طلاق کا ارادہ کیا۔ حضرت عمر نے دونوں کے درمیان تفریق کرادی (ب) حضرت عائشہ فرماتی ہیں کہ اللہ اور اس کے رسول نے ہمیں اختیار دیا۔ ہم نے اللہ اور اس کے رسول کو اختیار کیا تو اس کی وجہ سے ہم پر کوئی طلاق شمار نہیں ہوئی ( ج) حضرت عائشہ نے فرمایا بنت جون جب رخصت ہو کر حضور کے پاس آئی اور حضور اس کے قریب ہوئے تو کہنے لگی میں آپ کے بارے میں اللہ سے پناہ چاہتی ہوں۔ آپ نے فرمایا تم نے بڑی ذات سے پناہ مانگی اس لئے اپنے اہل کے پاس چلی جاؤ۔
If he has no intention of divorce, then by these words no divorce occurs, except if they are in a discussion of divorce, then divorce occurs in the judicial ruling. He came to Umar ibn al-Khattab, and he made him swear between the Rukn and the Maqam: 'What did you intend by your statement?' He said: 'I intended divorce.' So he separated them. (a) (Sunan al-Bayhaqi, bab ma ja'a fi kinayat al-talaq allati la yqa'u al-talaq biha illa an yurid makhraj al-kalam minhu al-talaq, j 7 p.562 no.15012; Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq, bab habluk ala gharik, j 6 p.370 no.11232) From this athar it is known that after intention divorce occurs. Also that it is a bain divorce, since Umar separated them. Also that 'habluk ala gharik' is among the kinayah words. If he does not intend, nothing occurs. The evidence is this hadith: from Aisha: she said, 'The Messenger of Allah gave us the choice, so we chose Allah and His Messenger, and he did not count anything as divorce against us.' (b) (Bukhari, bab man khayyar ajazwaj, p.791 no.5262; Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq, bab idh hmi fatimi, j 6 p.366 no.11214) In this hadith, the word 'ikhtiyar' is a kinayah, but in the hadith they did not intend divorce, so no divorce occurred. [1909](25) Therefore, if there is no intention of divorce, divorce does not occur with these words, unless they are in a discussion of divorce, then divorce occurs in the judicial ruling, and it does not occur between him and Allah, unless he intends divorce. Using kinayah words without intention: divorce does not occur. However, if the discussion of divorce was going on and the style was such that he wanted to give divorce by kinayah, then in the judicial ruling divorce occurs. But between the husband and Allah, if he intended, divorce occurs; if he did not, it does not. An incident of discussion of divorce is that of Amrat bint al-Jawn: she sought refuge, and the Prophet (peace be upon him) did not touch her, so he said the kinayah word 'al-haqi bi-ahlik'. Since the environment was a discussion of divorce, divorce occurred. And here there was intention to divorce, so divorce occurred. The hadith is from Aisha: 'When the daughter of al-Jawn was brought to the Messenger of Allah and he approached her, she said: I seek refuge with Allah from you. He said: You have sought refuge with the Mighty; go to your family.' (c) (Bukhari, bab man tallaq wa hal yuwajihu al-rajulu imra'atahu bi al-talaq, p.790 no.) Footnote: (a) A man said to his wife 'your rope is on your shoulder' multiple times. Then he came to Umar ibn al-Khattab. He made him swear between the Rukn and the Maqam: 'What did you intend by your statement?' The man said: 'I intended divorce.' Umar separated them. (b) Aisha said: Allah and His Messenger gave us choice; we chose Allah and His Messenger, so no divorce was counted against us. (c) Aisha said: When Bint al-Jawn was brought to the Prophet and he approached her, she said: I seek refuge with Allah from you. He said: You have sought refuge with the Mighty, so go to your family.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage addresses the case when a husband uses a kinayah word without any intention of divorce. The ruling: no divorce occurs, as the essential element of intention is missing. However, there is an exception: if the words are uttered in the context of discussing divorce (muthakarah al-talaq), then the court (qada) will rule that a divorce has taken place, due to the circumstances indicating intent. But as far as the husband's relationship with Allah is concerned, if he had no inner intention, then no divorce is considered. The author supports this with several proofs. First, the incident of a man who said 'habluk ala gharik' (your rope is on your hump) multiple times; he went to Umar, who made him swear by Allah between the Rukn and Maqam, and when he admitted intention, Umar separated them. This shows that intention is key. Second, the hadith of Aisha that the Prophet (peace be upon him) gave his wives the choice (ikhtiyar); they chose Allah and His Messenger, and no divorce was counted, even though 'ikhtiyar' is a kinayah word—because they did not intend divorce. Third, the incident of Bint al-Jawn: she sought refuge, and the Prophet said 'al-haqi bi-ahlik' (go to your family). Since this was in a context where divorce was being discussed (she refused him), and the Prophet intended divorce, it was considered a divorce. The commentary therefore clarifies that kinayah words require both intention and sometimes context for judicial validity. All examples are from Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
مذاكرة الطلاق (muthakarah al-talaq): A discussion or conversation about divorce, indicating that the speakers have divorce in mind.
قضاء (qada): Judicial ruling or court judgment; divorce that is effective in the eyes of the court even if the husband claims no inner intention.
فيمابينه و بين الله (fima baynahu wa bayna Allah): Between him and Allah; the inner reality and accountability before God, as opposed to worldly court rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says 'Go to your family' during an argument about divorce, but he has no inner intention to divorce, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) No divorce occurs at all.
B) Divorce occurs only in the court's ruling, but not between him and Allah.
C) One bain divorce occurs because of the context.
D) Three divorces occur because of the kinayah word.

Key Takeaway
Kinayah divorce depends on intention; the court may infer intention from context, but before Allah only true intention counts.

Divorce in Anger without Discussing Divorce
Rulings on divorce in anger context when not discussing divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on divorce pronouncements made in anger when the couple is not discussing divorce, and distinguish between words intended as abuse and those not intended as abuse.

Core Idea
In anger, words not meant as abuse effect divorce; words meant as abuse do not, unless intention for divorce is present.

Understanding how context affects the legal effect of ambiguous divorce pronouncements is crucial in Hanafi fiqh. This passage from al-Sharh al-Thamiri clarifies that when a couple is in a state of anger but not discussing divorce, the nature of the words spoken determines whether a divorce occurs. The key principle is that words which are not typically used as insults are presumed to be divorce pronouncements, while words that are clearly abusive are not taken as divorce unless the husband explicitly intends divorce. This ruling contrasts with Imam al-Shafi'i's position on kinayah words.

Guided Path
1
Identify the context
The passage specifies two contexts: (a) the general rule requires intention for divorce, and (b) the special context of anger and not discussing divorce modifies the ruling. Confirm that the couple is not in a discussion of divorce and are in anger or enmity.
The general rule is stated in the first line: 'It does not occur between him and Allah except that he intends it.'
2
Classify the word pronounced
Determine whether the word is intended as abuse or not. Words that are not typically abusive (e.g., 'you are divorced', 'you are free') are considered non-abusive. Words that are clearly insults (e.g., 'you dog', 'you fool') are considered abusive.
The passage uses 'sabb' and 'shatimah' for abuse and insult.
3
Apply the ruling for non-abusive words
If the word is not intended as abuse, divorce occurs regardless of the husband's actual intention. The anger context provides the presumption that the husband means divorce.
Principle: words from which divorce is inferred effect divorce.
4
Apply the ruling for abusive words
If the word is intended as abuse, divorce does NOT occur unless the husband also intends divorce. The anger explains the abuse, not a divorce attempt.
Intention becomes decisive for abusive words.
5
Note the Shafi'i difference
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that all kinayah words (including those not explicit) produce a raj'i divorce regardless of context. The Hanafi school differentiates based on context and nature of words.
The thamarah al-khilaf: under Hanafi, divorce may be bain; under Shafi'i, it is always raj'i unless three intended.

Commentary
This passage from al-Sharh al-Thamiri presents the rulings on divorce pronouncements during anger or argument when the couple is not specifically discussing divorce. The text begins by stating that if the husband pronounces words that could be divorce but without explicit intention, it does not count between him and Allah unless he intends it. However, in the context of anger or enmity, the ruling changes: any word that is not intended as abuse will effect a divorce because the circumstances indicate the intention for divorce. Conversely, if the word is clearly intended as abuse (like cursing), it will not be taken as divorce unless the husband explicitly intends divorce. The commentary explains the rationale using the principle that contextual clues determine the legal effect of ambiguous words. It also mentions a hadith where the phrase 'I seek refuge in Allah from you' was understood as a divorce in a specific context. Additionally, there is a point of disagreement with Imam al-Shafi'i: he holds that kinayah words only produce a raj'i (revocable) divorce, while the Hanafi school considers the context to determine whether the divorce is raj'i or bain. In this passage, the Hanafi view is that the nature of the divorce depends on the words and circumstances.

Key Terms
مذاكرة الطلاق (mudhakarat al-talaq): Discussion or conversation specifically about divorce.
کنایہ (kinayah): Words that can imply divorce but are not explicit; their effect depends on context or intention.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Divorce occurs only if they were previously discussing divorce.

In anger without discussing divorce, non-abusive words effect divorce; abusive words do not unless intention for divorce is present.

Intensifying Divorce Leads to Bain Divorce
Ruling that describing divorce with intensifiers makes it bain (irrevocable)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on intensifying phrases in divorce and distinguish it from the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
Adding intensifiers to a divorce pronouncement changes it from raj'i to bain.

This lesson examines how the language a husband uses in divorce can alter the legal consequence. While a simple 'you are divorced' yields one raj'i divorce, adding words like 'bain' or 'most severe' triggers an irrevocable bain divorce. We will study the evidence from the Companions and contrast the Hanafi view with that of Imam al-Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The core act is pronouncing divorce (talaq) with an added intensifying phrase. The Hanafi ruling is that this changes the divorce from raj'i (revocable) to bain (irrevocable).
The base ruling: simple explicit divorce gives one raj'i divorce.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the athar (narration) from Ali and Aisha regarding the phrase 'Haml ba'ir' (a load of a camel). The ruling was that the woman becomes unlawful until she marries another man.
This athar is cited as evidence for the bain nature.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The phrase 'Haml ba'ir' is an intensifier. The athar indicates that such an expression results in a complete separation, which in Hanafi terminology is bain divorce.
The wajh: intensifiers imply finality, thus bain.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, arguing that since divorce is disliked, intensifiers should not increase the effect; the divorce should remain raj'i.
Shafi'i position: intensifiers do not change the type.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi school adopts its position based on the athar and the principle that the husband's addition indicates a desire for finality.
Mufta bihi: bain divorce occurs with intensifiers.

(29) For example, he says (1) you are bain-divorced, (2) you are very severe divorced, (3) you are worst divorced, (4) upon you is the divorce of shaytan, (5) upon you is the divorce of innovation, (6) or equal to a mountain, (7) or like filling the house. In all these words, there are intensifying words with divorce, so bain divorce occurs. The proof has passed in the previous athar. Imam al-Shafi'i says that divorce is disliked, so even with intensifying words, only raj'i divorce occurs.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage deals with the effect of adding intensifying phrases to explicit divorce words. The base ruling is that explicit pronouncements like 'you are divorced' effect one raj'i divorce. However, if the husband adds a phrase that intensifies the divorce, such as 'bain' or 'most severe', the divorce becomes bain (irrevocable) instead of raj'i. The commentary provides examples from the hadith of Ali and Aisha, where a man divorced with 'Haml ba'ir' (a load of a camel) and the ruling was that she becomes unlawful until she marries another man, indicating a bain divorce. Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, arguing that because divorce is disliked, intensifiers should not change the nature; it should remain raj'i. The Hanafi view, supported by the athar, is that the husband's addition shows his desire for a final separation, so the divorce becomes bain.

Key Terms
بائن (ba'in): An irrevocable divorce that ends the marriage immediately; the wife becomes unlawful without a new marriage contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) No divorce occurs because it is unclear.

Key Takeaway
Adding intensifiers to a divorce pronouncement changes the divorce from raj'i to bain in Hanafi fiqh.

Valid Divorce by Attributing to the Whole Body or Representative Parts
Ruling that attributing divorce to a part representing the whole is valid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on attributing divorce to a limb that represents the whole (e.g., neck) and explain the Quranic proof used.

Core Idea
Attributing divorce to a limb that represents the whole is valid based on Quranic usage of 'raqabah'.

In Hanafi fiqh, the pronouncement of divorce is not limited to naming the wife directly. If a husband says 'your neck is divorced' or 'your body is divorced,' such statements are legally effective. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this ruling, showing how Arabic usage and a Quranic verse support treating a part as standing for the whole.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Type of Pronouncement
Read the divorce phrases in the passage (e.g., 'your neck is divorced', 'your soul is divorced'). Recognize that some refer to the whole body directly, while others use a limb to represent the whole.
The passage lists multiple examples for clarity.
2
Understand the Concept of Representative Limb
Note that in Arabic, certain body parts like 'neck' (raqabah) are idiomatically used to refer to the whole person. This is a customary usage known as 'urf.
Other parts include face, private parts, etc.
3
Locate the Quranic Proof
The passage cites Surah Nisa 4:92 where 'freeing a believing neck' means freeing a slave (the whole person). This proves that attributing an action to a part can be valid legally.
The verse: و من قتل مؤمنا خطأ فتحرير رقبة مؤمنة
4
Apply the Ruling to Other Limbs
The same logic applies to any limb that in customary usage stands for the whole person, e.g., 'your face is divorced' or 'your private parts are divorced'.
The passage explicitly includes these examples.

(۱۹۱۴) (۳۰) اگر منسوب کیا طلاق کو کل کی طرف یا ایسے عضو کی طرف جس سے کل کی تعبیر ہوتی ہو تو طلاق واقع ہوگی۔ طلاق کو کل جسم کی طرف منسوب کیا یا ایسے عضو کی طرف منسوب کیا جس سے کل جسم کی تعبیر کرتے ہیں تب بھی طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی۔ آیت اور حدیث کے محاورے میں تعبیر کرنے کا انداز دونوں ہیں کہ کسی چیز کو کل کی طرف منسوب کرتے ہیں اور کسی چیز کو ایسے عضو کی طرف منسوب کرتے ہیں جس سے پورا جسم مراد لیا جاتا ہو۔ مثال اور دلیل آگے آ رہی ہے۔ [۱۹۱۵] (۳۱) مثلا یوں کہے (۱) تو طلاق والی ہے (۲) تیری گردن کو (۳) تیری گردن کو (۴) تیری روح کو (۵) تیرے بدن کو (۶) تیرے جسم کو (۷) یا تیری شرم گاہ کو (۸) تیرے چہرے کو طلاق ہے۔ تو طلاق والی ہے اس میں پورے جسم کی طرف طلاق منسوب کی۔ اور رقبتک طالق میں اس کے ایسے عضو کی طرف منسوب کی جس سے پورا جسم مراد لیتے ہیں۔ اسی طرح دوسرے اعضاء بھی ہیں۔ رقبتہ بول کر پورا جسم مراد لینے کا ثبوت اس آیت میں ہے۔ ومـن قـتـل مـؤمـنـا خطاء فتحرير رقبة مؤمنة (ج) (آيت ٩٢ سورة النساء)
1914 (30) And if he adds divorce to her whole or to what expresses the whole, divorce occurs. 1915 (31) Examples: such as saying 'you are divorced', 'your neck is divorced', 'your throat is divorced', 'your soul is divorced', 'your body', 'your frame', 'or your private parts'. (a) (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah...) (3) from Aisha concerning a man who divorced his wife with one like a calf, she said: 'She is not lawful for him until she marries another husband.' (b) From these narrations it is known that from adding intensifying words, a mughallazah divorce can also occur. (30) If divorce is attributed to the whole or to a limb that is used to express the whole, divorce occurs. If divorce is attributed to the whole body or to a limb by which the whole body is meant, then divorce also occurs. The usage in Quran and hadith is both: attributing something to the whole and attributing to a limb from which the whole body is intended. The example and proof come ahead. (31) For example, he says (1) you are divorced, (2) your neck is divorced, (3) your neck (repeated), (4) your soul is divorced, (5) your body is divorced, (6) your frame is divorced, (7) or your private parts are divorced, (8) your face is divorced. In 'you are divorced', divorce is attributed to the whole body. And in 'your neck is divorced', it is attributed to a limb by which the whole body is meant. Similarly for other limbs. The proof that 'neck' is used for the whole body is in the verse: 'And whoever kills a believer by mistake, then the freeing of a believing neck [slave]' (Quran 4:92).
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling when divorce is attributed not just to the wife by name but to her entire body or to a specific part that stands for the whole. The Hanafi position is that such pronouncements are valid and effect divorce. For example, 'your neck is divorced' is considered a valid divorce because in Arabic usage, 'neck' can refer to the whole person. The proof is from the Quranic verse where 'freeing a neck' means freeing a slave (the whole person). The commentary cites hadith from Aisha and Ali to support that such expressions result in a binding divorce. This ruling is consistent with the Hanafi method of interpreting words according to customary usage (urf).

Key Terms
رقبة (raqabah): Literally 'neck', but used idiomatically to mean the whole person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Ruling on Adding Intensifying Words to Talaq
Ruling with proof and inter-madhhab dispute on intensifying talaq phrases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on intensifying talaq phrases, explain the dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i, and apply the evidence from the athar of Ali and Aisha.

Core Idea
Adding intensifying words to talaq makes it irrevocable (bain) in the Hanafi school.

When a husband pronounces divorce, the default ruling in the Hanafi school is that it is revocable (raj'i) unless he makes it clear he intends finality. This passage addresses a common scenario where the husband uses severe language such as 'the most severe divorce' or 'the ugliest divorce.' The Hanafi school, relying on the practice of the Companions Ali and Aisha, holds that such intensifying expressions transform the talaq into an irrevocable divorce (bain). Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, arguing that because talaq is disliked, adding extra words should not change its revocable nature. By studying this lesson, you will understand the Hanafi position, its proof, and the practical consequence of the disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the intensifying words and the Hanafi ruling
List the examples of intensifying phrases given in the text (e.g., 'ashad al-talaq', 'afhash al-talaq') and note that the Hanafi school rules that these result in bain talaq.
The key is that the words indicate finality.
2
Analyze the Hanafi proof (dalil)
The Hanafi evidence comes from athar (sayings) of Ali and Aisha. Read the footnotes: in two cases where severe expressions were used, the Companions ruled that the woman is not lawful to the husband until she marries another man. This clinches the bain status.
These athar are recorded in the footnote of the text.
3
State the Shafi'i counter-position and its basis
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that talaq remains raj'i even with intensifying words because talaq is fundamentally disliked, so nothing should increase its severity beyond the minimum.
The Shafi'i argument is based on the general dislike of divorce (al-talaq mabghud).
4
Examine the Hanafi response and thamarah al-khilaf
The Hanafi school responds by pointing to the explicit practice of the Companions: language that clearly indicates finality overrides the default. The practical consequence (thamarah) is that under the Hanafi school the wife is immediately forbidden and cannot return without a new marriage, while under the Shafi'i school the husband can take her back during the iddah.
This difference has a major impact on the couple's legal options.
5
Determine the mufta bihi position
The text presents the Hanafi position as the correct one, with the athar as strong evidence. In Hanafi fatwa, intensifying words lead to bain talaq.
This is the operative ruling for Hanafi muftis today.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
1913 (29) مثل ان يقول انت طالق بائن وانت طالق اشد الطلاق او افحش الطلاق او طلاق الشيطان او طلاق البدعة او كالجبل او ملأ البيت. ان تمام الفاظ میں طلاق کے ساتھ شدت کے الفاظ ہیں اس لئے ان سے طلاق بائنہ واقع ہوگی۔ دلیل او پر اثر گزر چکا ہے۔ امام شافعی فرماتے ہیں کہ طلاق مبغوض ہے اس لئے شدت کے الفاظ لگانے سے بھی طلاق رجعی ہی واقع ہوگی۔
حاشیہ : (الف) حضرت علی نے فرمایا ایک آدمی نے اپنی بیوی کو حمل بغیر کی طلاق دی۔ فرمایا جب تک دوسری شادی نہ کرے پہلے کے لئے حلال نہیں (ب) حضرت عائشہ نے فرمایا کسی آدمی نے بیوی کو ہزار کی طرح ایک طلاق دی، فرمایا اس کے لئے حلال نہیں ہے جب تک دوسری شادی نہ کرے
Like if he says: You are divorcing bain, and you are divorcing the most severe divorce, or the ugliest divorce, or the divorce of shaitan, or the divorce of innovation, or like a mountain, or filling the house. In all these words, there are words of severity with talaq, therefore bain talaq will occur. The proof has passed on the effect. Imam Shafi'i says that talaq is disliked, therefore even with adding words of severity, raj'i talaq will occur. Footnote: (a) Ali said: A man divorced his wife like a pregnant camel. He said: She is not lawful for him until she marries another husband. (b) Aisha said: A man gave his wife one divorce like a thousand. She said: She is not lawful for him until she marries another.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and an inter-madhhab dispute about adding intensifying words to talaq. The matn gives examples of phrases like 'you are divorced bain', 'most severe divorce', 'divorce of shaitan', etc. The commentator explains that because these words include severity, they result in bain divorce (irrevocable divorce). The proof is given by athar (sayings of Ali and Aisha) which show that severe expressions make the divorce final. Imam Shafi'i disagrees, arguing that talaq is disliked, so adding intensifying words should still result in raj'i divorce (revocable). The Hanafi position is that the intent of severity makes it bain.

Key Terms
طلاق بائن (talaq bain): A type of divorce that is irrevocable, severing the marriage tie immediately without possibility of return unless a new marriage (with another) occurs.
تقليد (tashdid): Intensification; adding words like 'ashad' (most severe) to the pronouncement of talaq to indicate finality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Raj'i talaq
B) Bain talaq
C) Neither
D) It depends on intention

Key Takeaway
Adding intensifying words to talaq makes it bain in the Hanafi school, based on the practice of the Companions.

Attributing Talaq to the Whole Body or Its Expression
Ruling with proof on attributing talaq to the whole body or a part expressing it

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling that attributing talaq to a body part representing the whole is valid, and identify the Quranic proof for this ruling.

Core Idea
In Arabic, a part can mean the whole – attributing talaq to such a part makes talaq effective.

This lesson examines a key Hanafi ruling on talaq: what happens when a husband does not explicitly say 'you are divorced' but instead says 'your neck is divorced'? The Hanafi school, relying on Quranic linguistic conventions, validates such talaq. We will see the proof from the Quran and how analogy extends to other body parts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is attributing talaq to the woman's entire body or to a part that idiomatically represents her. The ruling is that talaq occurs (sahih).
2
Locate the dalil (evidence)
The author cites three Quranic verses: an-Nisa 92 (raqaba), ash-Shu'ara 4 (a'naq), and Taha 111 (wujuh).
Each uses a body part to refer to the whole person.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (how the proof proves the ruling)
The words 'raqaba', 'a'naq', and 'wujuh' are used in the Quran to denote the entire person, establishing that certain body parts can represent the whole. This linguistic convention validates attributing talaq to such parts.
The proof is from Quranic usage, which is authoritative in establishing linguistic conventions.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
There is no disagreement; the ruling is mutually agreed upon (muttafaq alayh) among Hanafis and others.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
This is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position from Zahir al-Riwayah.
No need for tarjih as no ikhtilaf.

حاشیہ : (الف) ان کی گردیں جھک گئیں (ب) چہرے کی قیوم کے سامنے جھک گئے (ج) کسی (باقی اگلے صفحہ پر)
And if he attributes talaq to her entirety or to what expresses entirety, talaq occurs. For example, if he says: you are divorced, or your neck is divorced, or your neck is divorced, or your soul is divorced, or your body, or your flesh, or your private part, or your face. He attributed talaq to the whole body, or to a limb by which the whole body is expressed, then also talaq will occur. The style of expression in the Quran and Hadith is both: sometimes attributing something to the whole, and sometimes to a limb that implies the whole body. Example and proof come later. For example, he says: (1) you are divorced, (2) your neck, (3) your neck, (4) your soul, (5) your body, (6) your flesh, (7) your private part, (8) your face. In 'you are divorced', talaq is attributed to the whole body. In 'your neck is divorced', it is attributed to a limb by which the whole body is understood. Similarly for other limbs. The proof of using 'neck' to mean whole body is in the verse: 'And whoever kills a believer by mistake, then the freeing of a believing neck' (Surah an-Nisa). And the proof of using 'neck' (another word) is in the verse: 'Then their necks became humbled to it' (Surah ash-Shu'ara 26). And the proof of using 'face' to mean whole body is in the verse: 'And faces will be humbled before the Living, the Self-Sustaining' (Surah Taha 20). The rest can be analogized to this.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof about attributing talaq to the woman's entire body or to a part that represents the whole. The matn states that if talaq is attributed to the whole (e.g., 'you are divorced') or to a part that idiomatically means the whole (like 'neck', 'soul', 'body', 'private part', 'face'), the divorce occurs. The commentator explains that this is based on Quranic usage: 'raqaba' (neck) means the whole person in the verse about freeing a slave; 'a'naq' (necks) means the whole person in the verse about humiliation; and 'wujuh' (faces) means the whole person in the verse about submission. The same applies analogically to other body parts that conventionally represent the whole. The footnote provides Urdu translations of the relevant Quranic verses.

Key Terms
جملة (jumla): The entirety of the woman's person, as opposed to a part.
ما يعبر به عن الجملة (ma yu'abbiru bihi 'an al-jumla): A part of the body (like neck, face) that idiomatically represents the whole person in Arabic.

Talaq on an Undifferentiated Part (Juz' Shai'i) of the Woman
Ruling with proof on talaq attributed to an undifferentiated part like half or third

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when talaq is attributed to an undivided part of the woman and explain the evidence from the athar and qiyas.

Core Idea
Talaq is indivisible; half a talaq is a full talaq, and half a woman is the whole for talaq.

Talaq is a complete dissolution of marriage; it cannot be partitioned. This lesson examines what happens when a husband attempts to divorce only part of his wife—like saying 'your half is divorced'. The Hanafi school rules that such a pronouncement effects a full talaq, and we will explore the evidence from the athar of al-Sha'bi and the underlying qiyas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the husband invoking talaq by attributing it to an undivided part (juz' shai'i) of the wife, e.g., half or third. The Hanafi ruling is that this constitutes a full, valid talaq affecting the whole woman.
The key term is 'juz' shai'i' – an undefined fraction not tied to a specific limb.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary proof is an athar from al-Sha'bi: if a man says 'you are half or a third divorced', it is one full divorce. This establishes the principle that talaq cannot be partitioned.
References: Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba and Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The athar treats fractional divorce as whole. By qiyas (analogy), attributing talaq to half the woman is equivalent to attributing it to her entire person, because the illa (cause) is that talaq is indivisible in its legal effect.
The 'illa is the indivisibility of talaq – it is either complete or absent.
4
Note the ikhtilaf and tarjih
This ruling is unanimously held within the Hanafi school (muttafaq alayh). There is no significant intra-school dispute. The position is mufta bihi (relied upon for fatwa) as it is based on clear athar and consistent qiyas.
Authority weight: zahir al-riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
1916 (32) و كذلک ان طلق جزء شائعا منها مثل ان يقول نصفك او ثلثك طالق. اسی طرح اگر عورت کے جزء شائع کو طلاق دی۔ مثلا یوں کہا تیرا نصف یا تیرا ثلث حصہ طلاق والا ہے۔ ا آدھا، تہائی، چوتھائی ان کو جزو شائع کہتے ہیں۔ اگر عورت کے جزء شائع کو طلاق دی تو پورے جسم پر سرایت ہوگی اور پورے انسان کو طلاق واقع ہوگی۔ آدھے جسم کو طلاق واقع ہوگی تو عورت کے ہر ہر عضو کے آدھے پر طلاق واقع ہوگی اس لئے طلاق میں آدھا نہیں ہوتا پورا ہی ہوتا ہے۔ اس لئے پورے انسان کو طلاق ہوگی (2) اثر میں ہے۔ عن الشعبي قال اذا قال انت طالق نصفا او ثلث تطليقة فهي تطليقة (مصنف ابن ابی شيبة 51 ما قالوا في الرجل يطلق امراته نصف تطليقة ج رابع ص 78 نمبر 18055 و مصنف عبدالرزاق نمبر 11251) اس اثر میں آدھی طلاق اور تہائی طلاق کو ایک طلاق قرار دیا۔ اس پر قیاس کر کے آدھے انسان کو پورا انسان قرار دیا جائے گا۔
And similarly, if he divorces a common part of her, like saying: your half or your third is divorced. Similarly, if he gives talaq to a common part of the woman, e.g., saying: your half or your third share is divorced. These are called juz' shai'i. If he gives talaq to a common part of the woman, it will permeate the whole body and talaq will occur to the entire person. If half the body is divorced, then half of every limb of the woman would be divorced, but talaq does not have halves; it is whole. Therefore, talaq will occur to the whole person. And there is an athar from al-Sha'bi: if he says 'you are divorced half or a third of a divorce', it is a full divorce. In this athar, half divorce and third divorce are considered one divorce. By analogy, half a person is considered a whole person.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof about talaq on an undifferentiated part (juz' shai'i) of the woman, such as half or third. The commentator explains that if a man says 'your half is divorced' or 'your third is divorced', this talaq affects the entire woman because talaq cannot be divided; it applies to the whole. The proof is an athar from al-Sha'bi: if someone says 'you are half or third divorced', it counts as one full divorce. By analogy (qiyas), attributing talaq to half of the woman is like attributing it to the whole.

Key Terms
جزء شائع (juz' shai'i): An undifferentiated part of the whole, like half, third, quarter, that is not specified to a particular organ.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says to his wife 'your half is divorced', what is the ruling according to the Hanafi school?
A) Half a talaq occurs, leaving the marriage partially intact
B) A full talaq occurs because talaq cannot be partitioned
C) No talaq occurs because it is incomplete
D) It depends on his intention

Key Takeaway
Attributing talaq to an undifferentiated part (juz' shai'i) of the wife results in a full talaq affecting the entire marriage, because talaq is indivisible by nature.

Divorcing an Undivided Part of the Wife
Ruling with proof on divorcing a fractional part

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why divorcing a fractional part of the wife constitutes a full divorce and apply the Hanafi principle of indivisibility of talaq.

Core Idea
Talaq is indivisible: divorcing half the wife divorces the whole.

In this lesson, we examine a specific ruling from the Book of Divorce: what happens when a husband utters a talaq tied to an undivided part of his wife, such as 'half of you is divorced'? The Hanafi school holds that such a statement triggers a full divorce on the entire wife. We will explore the legal proof (dalil) and the reasoning (qiyas) that supports this ruling, and clarify why talaq cannot be partial.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the husband's pronouncement of talaq on an undivided part (juz' shaa'i') of the wife, e.g., 'half of you' or 'a third of you'. The ruling (hukm) is that a full talaq occurs on the entire wife.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is an athar (report) from the Tabi'i al-Sha'bi: 'If a man says to his wife: You are divorced half or a third of a divorce, it is one divorce.' This shows that fractional talaq expressions count as full talaq.
The athar is recorded in Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah and Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The explicit statement equates half or a third of a talaq to a full talaq. This indicates that talaq cannot be fractional; the expression is interpreted as intending a complete talaq.
4
Apply qiyas to the case
By analogy (qiyas), if a fractional talaq counts as a full talaq, then divorcing half the person also applies to the whole person. The ruling permeates the entire being because talaq is indivisible.
5
Confirm the Hanafi position
The mufta bihi (operative position) is that divorcing an undivided part results in a valid and effective talaq on the whole wife. This is the standard Hanafi view from the zahir al-riwayah.

Source Text

و کذلک ان طـلـق جـزء شائعا منها مثل ان يقول نصفک او ثلثک طالق
[۱۹۱۶] (۳۲) اسی طرح اگر عورت کے جزء شائع کو طلاق دی۔ مثلا یوں کہا تیرا نصف یا تیرا ثلث حصہ طلاق والا ہے۔ ا آدھا، نہائی ، چوتھائی ان کو جزو شائع کہتے ہیں۔ اگر عورت کے جزء شائع کو طلاق دی تو پورے جسم پر سرایت ہوگی اور پورے انسان کو طلاق واقع ہوگی۔ آدھے جسم کو طلاق واقع ہوگی تو عورت کے ہر ہر عضو کے آدھے پر طلاق واقع ہوگی اس لئے طلاق میں آدھا نہیں ہوتا پورا ہی ہوتا ہے۔ اس لئے پورے انسان کو طلاق ہوگی (۲) اثر میں ہے۔ عن الشعبي قال اذا قال انت طالق نصفا او ثلث تطليقة فهي تطليقة ( ج ) ( مصنف ابن ابی شیته ۵۱ ما قالوافی الرجل يطلق امرأته نصف تطلیقہ ج رابع ص ۷ ۸ نمبر ۱۸۰۵۵ار مصنف عبدالرزاق نمبر ۱۱۲۵۱) اس اثر میں آدھی طلاق اور تہائی طلاق کو ایک طلاق قرار دیا۔ اس پر قیاس کر کے آدھے انسان کو پورا انسان قرار دیا جائے گا۔
And similarly, if he divorces a common part (shaa'i') of her, such as he says: 'Half of you' or 'One-third of you is divorced.' [1916] (32) Similarly, if one divorces an undivided part of the woman — for example, says: 'Your half' or 'your third share is divorced' — i.e., half, third, quarter are called undivided parts. If one divorces an undivided part of the woman, it will permeate the entire body and divorce will occur to the whole person. If half the body is divorced, divorce will occur on half of every organ of the woman. Therefore, divorce does not become half; it is always whole. Hence the entire person is divorced. (2) There is an athar: from al-Sha'bi who said: 'If a man says to his wife: You are divorced half or a third of a divorce, it is one divorce.' (c) (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah, chapter on what they said about a man divorcing his wife half a divorce, vol. 4, p. 87, no. 18055; Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq, no. 11251). In this athar, half a divorce and a third of a divorce are considered one divorce. By analogy upon this, half of a person is considered the whole person.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage states a ruling from the Book of Divorce (Talaq). The matn says: if a man divorces an undivided part (juz' shaa'i') of his wife, such as saying 'half of you' or 'one-third of you is divorced,' the divorce takes effect on the entire wife. The commentator explains that an undivided part is a fraction like half, third, or quarter. When such a part is mentioned, it permeates the whole body because talaq is indivisible. The proof is an athar from the great Tabi'i al-Shu'bi, who said that saying 'half a divorce' or 'a third of a divorce' counts as one full divorce. By analogy (qiyas), half the body is treated as the whole body for talaq purposes. This ruling is consistent with the Hanafi principle that talaq cannot be partial; it either applies fully or not at all.

Key Terms
جزء شائع (juz' shaa'i'): An undivided or common part, such as half, third, or quarter of an entity
طلاق (talaq): The dissolution of marriage by the husband's pronouncement

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says to his wife, 'Half of you is divorced,' what is the ruling according to the Hanafi school?
A) No divorce occurs because half cannot be divorced.
B) One full divorce occurs on the entire wife.
C) Half a divorce occurs.
D) The marriage is invalidated without divorce.

Key Takeaway
Talaq is indivisible: divorcing an undivided part of the wife divorces the entire wife.
